The Condition of Specific Composition and Division of the Path
Author explains the need for specific composition and the division into definition and proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why specific composition (ta'lif) is necessary and classify a logical tool as either an explanatory statement (qawl sharh) or a proof (hujja).

Core Idea
The right material is useless without the right structure – both define sound reasoning.

Having established that prior appropriate knowledge is necessary, the author now emphasizes that this knowledge must be composed in a specific way. He uses the powerful analogy of a bed: the wood (material) and the shape (form) are both required. Then he divides the path into two exclusive categories: explanatory statement for conceptualization and proof for confirmation. Finally, he notes that this capacity is not innate but abstracted from sensory experience.

Guided Path
1
Identify the material and form in the analogy
The author compares prior appropriate meanings to wood (material) and the specific composition to the bed's form. Understand that both are necessary.
2
Recognize the necessity of specific composition
The author argues that any composition will not do; it must be a specific arrangement. For example, 'animal' and 'rational' must be combined in a certain way to define 'human'.
3
Distinguish between qawl sharh and hujja
The path divides: whatever leads to conceptualization is called qawl sharh (explanatory statement, i.e., definition); whatever leads to confirmation is called hujja (proof). These are distinct and not interchangeable.
4
Understand the origin of logical reasoning
The author concludes that this ability is not innate but extracted from sensory experience through abstraction.

Divisions of Verbal Signification: Correspondence, Inclusion, and Refutation of Implication
Author defines two types of verbal signification and refutes a third proposed type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between correspondence (mutabaqa) and inclusion (tadammun) and explain why implication (iltizam) is not a true verbal signification.

Core Idea
Not every associated meaning is signified; only correspondence and inclusion are true verbal significations.

Words signify meanings in two direct ways: either the whole meaning (correspondence) or a part of it (inclusion). Some propose a third type, implication, where a word suggests something external to its meaning. The author argues that implication is not a genuine verbal signification because it depends on mental association, not linguistic convention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two accepted types
Read the first part of the passage: the author defines correspondence (dalalat al-mutabaqa) as the word indicating its entire meaning exactly, and inclusion (dalalat al-tadammun) as indicating a part of that meaning. Note the example of 'house' signifying the whole structure (correspondence) and the ceiling alone (inclusion).
The example uses 'bayt' (house) – a concept familiar in Islamic contexts for defining property or inheritance.
2
Understand the stronger signification
The author states that correspondence is stronger because it does not require additional reasoning; inclusion depends on recognizing that the part is constitutive of the whole.
This distinction is important in legal interpretation where direct wording (correspondence) is prioritized.
3
Analyze the refutation of implication
The author rejects implication (iltizam) as a genuine division. He argues that if someone says 'human', the capacity to write or laugh is understood only through mental transition from the signified (rational animal) to its necessary concomitants. If that transition were absent, the implication would not be understood.
The key test: if understanding depends on a mental leap, it is not verbal signification.
4
Compare implication with inclusion
The author distinguishes implication from inclusion: inclusion involves a part of the meaning, while implication involves something external. Also, implication can occur for simple terms (e.g., 'tool') whereas inclusion requires a composite.
This shows that implication is broader in scope but not a direct signification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول: ما وقع مدلولاً للفظ - أي مفهوماً - إما نفس هوية موضوعه بالوضع الأصلي، أو العرف الاستعمالي، من غير تغيير وإما جزء موضوعه، فما مثل الدلالة الأولى تسمى (دلالة المطابقة) لما فيه من وضع اللفظ على معناه من غير زيادة ولا نقصان، وهي كدلالة البيت على مجموع السقف والحامل له، وما مثل الدلالة الثانية تسمى دلالة (التضمن) وهي كدلالة البيت على ما هو داخل في معناه من وحده، أو الحامل وحده، من حيث هو داخل في المدلول لا على وجه يكون اللفظ موضوعاً له وحده، فإنه إذ ذاك مطابق ودلالته عليه، إما بجهة الاشتراك أو التجوّز، وأقوى الدلالتين إنما هي المطابقة لاستغنائها في فهم مدلولها عن توسط أمرٍ خارج غير معرفة الوضع والاصطلاح في اللفظ المشار إليه على معناه، ولا كذلك في التضمن، فإن ما وقع مفهوماً منه متوقف على فهم كونه داخلاً في الموضوع ومقوماً له، وليس من ضرورة فهم كون الشيء موضوعاً للفظ تصور ما له من الذاتيات؛ لجواز أن يكون تصوره إنما هو بخواصه ولوازمه لا بذاتياته ومقوماته، وقد تفارق المطابقة للتضمن من وجهين آخرين [الوجه الأول: أن المطابقة أعم من التضمن فإن التضمن قد لا يلزم المطابقة، وهو إذا لم يكن لموضوع اللفظ المطابق جزء كلفظ (الآلة) والمطابقة على لازمه للتضمن دائماً. الوجه الثاني: أن الدلالة في المطابقة على موضوع اللفظ وفي التضمن على بعضه، ومن جعل دلالة الالتزام قسماً من أقسام الدلالات اللفظية فقد أخطأ؛ إذ معنى الالتزام هو أن يدل اللفظ على معنى وذلك المعنى يلزمه أمر خارج عن هويته، كملازمة استعداد الكتابة، والضحك للحيوان الناطق، الذي هو مدلول لفظ الإنسان، وهذه ليست دلالة لفظية، فإنه لم يكن اللازم مدلولاً للفظ، ولا داخلاً في مدلوله، وحيث وقع فهمه عند إطلاق اللفظ لم يكن إلا لانتقال الذهن مما دل عليه اللفظ إلى ما لزمه، حتى لو قدرنا ارتفاع هذا الانتقال لم يكن اللازم مفهوماً، ولو كان مستفاداً من اللفظ لما ارتفع العلم به عند فرض عدم انتقال الذهن إليه. ولا يلزم على هذا أن تكون دلالة التضمن غير لفظية من جهة توقف المدلول فيها على نظر عقليّ، فإنه جزء من الموضوع وداخل فيه، فإنما هو لتحقق كونه داخلاً في مدلول اللفظ، وعدم فهمه عند إطلاق اللفظ إن كان فلا يخرجه عن أن يكون مدلولاً للفظ وداخلاً فيه، بل هو كما لو جهلت دلالة اللفظ على نفس الموضوع، خلافاً لما في دلالة الالتزام فإنه لم يكن اللازم مفهوماً من اللفظ، ولا النظر العقلي في كونه داخلاً فيه. ومما تفارق به هذه الدلالة للدلالات اللفظية أن المدلول فيها خارج عن مدلول اللفظ، وأنه قد يتسلسل إلى أوّل مبدأ، وسواء كان خفيّاً أو جلياً، ومما تفارق به التضمن أنها قد تكون حيث لا تضمن إذ اللوازم قد تكون للبسائط كما تكون للمركبات فهي أعم منها.
We say: What occurs as a signified of an expression – i.e., a concept – is either the very essence of its subject according to original convention or customary usage, without change, or a part of its subject. So the first type of signification is called 'signification of correspondence' (dalalat al-mutabaqa) due to the word being placed on its meaning without increase or decrease, like the signification of 'house' for the totality of the ceiling and its support. The second type is called 'signification of inclusion' (dalalat al-tadammun), like the signification of 'house' for what is within its meaning, such as the ceiling alone or the support alone, in terms of being included in the signified, not in such a way that the word is placed for it alone – for then it would be correspondence, and its signification upon it would be either by homonymy or metaphor. The stronger of the two significations is correspondence because it suffices in understanding its signified without mediation of something external other than knowledge of the convention and usage of the word referred to for its meaning. Not so for inclusion, for what occurs as a concept from it depends on understanding that it is included in the subject and constitutive of it. And it is not necessary for understanding that a thing is the subject of a word to conceive its essentials; because it is possible that its conception is through its properties and accidentals, not through its essentials and constituents. And correspondence differs from inclusion in two other respects: [The first: correspondence is more general than inclusion, for inclusion may not entail correspondence, which is when the subject of the corresponding word has no part, like the word 'tool' – and correspondence upon its necessary concomitant is for inclusion always? The second: signification in correspondence is upon the subject of the word, and in inclusion upon some of it. And whoever makes signification of implication (iltizam) a division of the divisions of verbal signification has erred; for the meaning of implication is that the word signifies a meaning, and that meaning necessitates an external thing outside its essence, like the necessary concomitance of the disposition to write and laughter for the rational animal, which is the signified of the word 'human'. And this is not a verbal signification, for the necessary concomitant is neither a signified of the word nor included in its signified. When its understanding occurs upon uttering the word, it is only due to the mind's transition from what the word signifies to what is necessitated by it. Even if we suppose the removal of this transition, the necessary concomitant would not be understood; and if it were derived from the word, knowledge of it would not cease upon assuming no mental transition. And this does not entail that inclusion is non-verbal due to the dependence of its signified on intellectual consideration, for it is part of the subject and included in it. Rather, it is to verify that it is included in the signified of the word, and if it is not understood upon utterance, that does not remove it from being a signified of the word and included in it. It is like if you do not know the word's signification for the subject itself, contrary to implication, for the necessary concomitant is neither understood from the word nor is the intellectual consideration about its being included in it. And among the ways this signification [implication] differs from verbal significations is that its signified is external to the signified of the word, and that it can chain back to a first principle, whether it is obscure or evident. And it differs from inclusion in that it can occur where there is no inclusion, since necessary concomitants can be for simple things just as they are for composites, so it is more general than it.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is definitional and critical. The author defines two types of verbal signification: mutabaqa (correspondence) where the word signifies its entire meaning, and tadammun (inclusion) where it signifies a part of that meaning. An example from the Islamic context: the word 'house' (bayt) signifies the entire structure (mutabaqa) and also signifies the ceiling (saqf) by inclusion, since the ceiling is part of the house. The author then argues that a proposed third type, dalalat al-iltizam (implication), is not a true verbal signification. For instance, the word 'human' (insan) implies the capacity for writing or laughter, but these are not signified by the word itself; they are understood only through mental transition from what the word signifies to its necessary concomitants. The author supports this by noting that if the mental transition were absent, the implication would not be understood, whereas verbal signification does not depend on such transition. He then distinguishes implication from inclusion, because in inclusion the part is actually within the meaning, while in implication the concomitant is external. Moreover, implication can exist even for simple things, while inclusion requires a composite. This passage is foundational in logic for understanding how words relate to their meanings.

Key Terms
دلالة المطابقة
دلالة التضمن
دلالة الالتزام

Key Takeaway
Correspondence and inclusion are the only true verbal significations; implication is merely a mental association, not a direct property of the word.

What Makes an Expression Simple (Mufrad)?
Author defines simple expression and clarifies the condition about parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether an expression is mufrad or murakkab by checking whether any of its parts independently indicate a meaning.

Core Idea
A simple expression is indivisible in meaning, no matter how complex its referent.

In this passage, the author defines the foundational distinction between simple and compound expressions. Understanding this distinction is crucial because it determines the basic units of meaning in logical analysis. Many students mistakenly think that a simple expression must have a simple meaning, but the author clarifies that even a word like 'Zayd'—which refers to a composite entity—is considered simple because its letters do not individually signify.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the Arabic term being defined: 'المفرد' (al-mufrad). Note that the author uses 'اللفظ المفرد' as the full phrase.
2
Extract the condition for mufrad
The key condition is: 'ولا دلالة لجزء منه على شيء أصلاً' (no part of it indicates anything at all). Also note that it does not matter if the meaning is simple or composite.
This condition is exclusive: any expression that fails this condition is compound.
3
Apply the test to examples
Check the examples: 'Zayd' and 'al-hayawan'. Confirm that their letters (like yā-dāl, ḥā-yā) do not individually mean anything related to the whole meaning. The author emphasizes that even if a letter can indicate a meaning in another context (e.g., 'yā' as a vocative), if the positor did not intend it here, it is not a part in this word.
This demonstrates that being a 'part' is relative to the wad' (conventional designation).
4
Account for wad'
The author explains that whether a letter is considered a part depends on the positor's intention. If the positor intended the letters to indicate something, they would not be parts of that word. This is why 'Abdullah' can be mufrad when used as a nickname: its parts are not intended to bear their original meanings.
This step resolves potential counterexamples.
5
Distinguish from compound
Contrast with murakkab: a compound has a part that indicates part of the meaning intended by the whole sentence. So the test for mufrad is: no part indicates any meaning at all.
This sets the boundary for the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في المفرد والمركب. واللفظ المفرد هو الدال بالوضع على معنى، ولا دلالة لجزء منه على شيء أصلاً، ولا تبالي كان ذلك المعنى بسيطاً كـ (النفس) و(العقل) أو مركباً كـ (زید) و (الحيوان) فإن جزءاً ما منهما ولتكن (الياء) و(الدال) من زيد، و(الحاء) و(الياء) من الحيوان لا يدل بهما على شيء، وحيث كانت (الياء) و(الدال) من زيد أو (الحاء) و(الـيـاء) من الحيوان يدل بهما على شيء لم يكونا إذ ذاك جزءاً من لفظ زيد أو الحيوان؛ لأن جعل بعض الألفاظ جزءاً للبعض أو ليس هو لذوات الألفاظ، وإلا فما كان جزءاً استحال أن لا يكون جزءاً وما لم يكن جزءاً استحال أن يصير جزءاً، وإنما ذلك منوط بجعل الواضع وإرادته، ونحن نعلم أن الواضع حيث جعل (الياء) و(الدال) من زيد جزءاً من لفظ زيد لـم يـرد بـه الدلالة على شيء من معنى زيد ولا على خارج عنه، وحيث جعله دالا على شيء مـن زيـد أو خارج عن زيد لم يرد أن يجعله جزءاً من لفظ زيد وعند هذا تبين أنه لا حاجة إلى ما زيد في حده من حيث هو جزؤه، إذ الشيء لا يتصور أن لا يكون جزءاً حين هو جزؤه، ولا حين هو جزء جزء، وبه أيضاً يظهر فساد رأي من خصص انتفاء دلالة الجزء على جزء من معنى الجملة إن كان ذلك ظناً منه بكونه دالاً على معنى خارج حالة جعله جزءاً، وعلى هذا عبد الله ونحوه أن جعل لقباً ولم يقصد بأجزائه الدلالة حالة الاجتماع على ما يقصد بها حالة الانفراد فمفرد وإلا فمركب. والمركب هو ما يوجد له جزء يدل على جزء من المعنى المراد بالجملة، وهو إما أن يستقل كل جزء من أجزائه بالدلالة أو لا يستقل فإن كان الأول فهو المركب التام، وهو إما أن يكون مركباً خبرياً أو ليس فإن كان مركباً خبرياً فلا يتصور تركبه من غير الأسماء كقولنا: زيد قائم أو من الأسماء والأفعال كقولنا: زيد قام أو
Chapter Two: On the Simple and the Compound. The simple expression is that which indicates, by designation, a meaning, and there is no indication of any part of it for anything at all, and it does not matter whether that meaning is simple like 'soul' and 'intellect' or compound like 'Zayd' and 'the animal', for a part of them, let it be the 'ya' and 'dal' of Zayd, and the 'ha' and 'ya' of 'al-hayawan', do not indicate anything by them. And when the 'ya' and 'dal' of Zayd or the 'ha' and 'ya' of 'al-hayawan' indicate something by them, they are not then a part of the word Zayd or 'al-hayawan'; because making some letters a part of others is not by virtue of the essences of the words, otherwise what is a part would be impossible not to be a part, and what is not a part would be impossible to become a part. Rather that depends on the positor's positioning and intention. And we know that the positor, when he made the 'ya' and 'dal' of Zayd a part of the word Zayd, did not intend by them indication of anything of Zayd's meaning nor of anything external to it. And when he made them indicative of something of Zayd or external to Zayd, he did not intend to make them a part of the word Zayd. And with this it becomes clear that there is no need for what is added in its definition from the perspective of being its part, for a thing cannot be conceived not to be a part when it is its part, nor when it is part of a part. And by this also appears the corruption of the opinion of one who specifies the non-indication of the part upon part of the meaning of the sentence, if that is due to his assumption that it indicates an external meaning in the state of being a part. And on this basis, 'Abdullah' and the like, when made a nickname and not intending its parts to indicate, in the state of composition, what they indicate in the state of isolation, then it is simple; otherwise it is compound. And the compound is that which has a part that indicates part of the meaning intended by the sentence. And it is either that each part of its parts is independent in indication or not independent. If the first, then it is the complete compound. And it is either a declarative compound or not. If it is a declarative compound, then it is inconceivable to be composed of other than nouns, like our saying: 'Zayd is standing', or from nouns and verbs like our saying: 'Zayd stood', or...
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage defines the fundamental distinction between simple (mufrad) and compound (murakkab) expressions, a key classification in logic because it determines the structure of propositions. The author begins by defining mufrad: it indicates a meaning by waḍʿ (conventional designation), and no part of it indicates anything. This holds whether the meaning is simple (e.g., 'soul', 'intellect') or composite (e.g., 'Zayd', 'the animal'). The letters of these words do not individually signify. The author then discusses a subtlety: if the letters of a word happen to have independent meanings in other contexts (e.g., 'yā' as a vocative particle), then in that word they are not considered parts because the wāḍiʿ (the one who coined the term) did not intend them to carry meaning. The author uses this to refute an opinion that restricts the non-indication of parts only to cases where the part does not indicate part of the sentence's meaning. Then he gives the example of 'Abdullah' used as a proper name (laqab): if the parts 'abd' and 'Allah' are not intended to bear their original meanings within the compound, the word is considered simple; otherwise it is compound. Finally, the author defines murakkab as having a part that indicates part of the meaning of the whole. He divides murakkab into tāmm (complete) and ghayr tāmm (incomplete): complete when each part independently indicates a meaning. He then subdivides tāmm into khabarī (declarative) and other types, with examples 'Zayd qā'im' and 'Zayd qāma'. The passage ends as he begins to discuss non-declarative compounds.

Key Terms
المفرد
المركب
الواضع
المركب التام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because it is a proper noun.
D) Because it is composed of letters.

Key Takeaway
A simple expression is defined not by the simplicity of its meaning, but by the fact that none of its parts independently signify.

Compound Expressions: Complete and Incomplete
Author classifies compounds into complete and incomplete with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any compound expression as complete (tāmm) or incomplete based on whether each part independently indicates a meaning.

Core Idea
A complete compound is one where every part carries independent meaning, like a full sentence.

After defining mufrad and murakkab, the author further divides compounds into those that are complete (tāmm) and those that are incomplete. This classification is important because it determines whether an expression can stand alone as a proposition (qadiyya) in logical analysis. The example of 'Abdullah' as a nickname illustrates a borderline case and reinforces the role of the positor's intention.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of murakkab
A compound (murakkab) is defined as an expression that has a part which indicates part of the meaning intended by the whole sentence.
This sets the stage for further classification.
2
Identify the criterion for tāmm
The author states: 'كل جزء من أجزائه يستقل بالدلالة' (each part of its parts is independent in indication). This is the key feature of a complete compound.
independence means the part can convey meaning on its own in a sentence context.
3
Distinguish tāmm from ghayr tāmm
If every part is independent, it is tāmm; otherwise, it is ghayr tāmm (incomplete). For example, 'Zayd qā'im' is tāmm because both words independently indicate meaning, whereas 'Kitāb Allāh' is ghayr tāmm because 'kitāb' requires 'Allāh' to complete the meaning.
The term 'ghayr tāmm' is implied by the negation of the condition.
4
Analyze the 'Abdullah example
The author uses 'Abdullah' to show the effect of wad': when used as a nickname, the parts are not intended to bear their original meanings, so the expression becomes mufrad, not compound. But if intended as 'servant of Allah', it is a compound. This highlights that classification depends on the positor's intention.
This example bridges the concepts of mufrad/murakkab and tāmm/ghayr tāmm.
5
Recognize subtypes of tāmm
The author mentions that tāmm compounds can be khabarī (declarative) or non-declarative. Examples of khabarī: 'Zayd qā'im' (nominal sentence) and 'Zayd qāma' (verbal sentence). Non-declarative types are not detailed in this passage.
This prepares for later discussions on propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في المفرد والمركب. واللفظ المفرد هو الدال بالوضع على معنى، ولا دلالة لجزء منه على شيء أصلاً، ولا تبالي كان ذلك المعنى بسيطاً كـ (النفس) و(العقل) أو مركباً كـ (زید) و (الحيوان) فإن جزءاً ما منهما ولتكن (الياء) و(الدال) من زيد، و(الحاء) و(الياء) من الحيوان لا يدل بهما على شيء، وحيث كانت (الياء) و(الدال) من زيد أو (الحاء) و(الـيـاء) من الحيوان يدل بهما على شيء لم يكونا إذ ذاك جزءاً من لفظ زيد أو الحيوان؛ لأن جعل بعض الألفاظ جزءاً للبعض أو ليس هو لذوات الألفاظ، وإلا فما كان جزءاً استحال أن لا يكون جزءاً وما لم يكن جزءاً استحال أن يصير جزءاً، وإنما ذلك منوط بجعل الواضع وإرادته، ونحن نعلم أن الواضع حيث جعل (الياء) و(الدال) من زيد جزءاً من لفظ زيد لـم يـرد بـه الدلالة على شيء من معنى زيد ولا على خارج عنه، وحيث جعله دالا على شيء مـن زيـد أو خارج عن زيد لم يرد أن يجعله جزءاً من لفظ زيد وعند هذا تبين أنه لا حاجة إلى ما زيد في حده من حيث هو جزؤه، إذ الشيء لا يتصور أن لا يكون جزءاً حين هو جزؤه، ولا حين هو جزء جزء، وبه أيضاً يظهر فساد رأي من خصص انتفاء دلالة الجزء على جزء من معنى الجملة إن كان ذلك ظناً منه بكونه دالاً على معنى خارج حالة جعله جزءاً، وعلى هذا عبد الله ونحوه أن جعل لقباً ولم يقصد بأجزائه الدلالة حالة الاجتماع على ما يقصد بها حالة الانفراد فمفرد وإلا فمركب. والمركب هو ما يوجد له جزء يدل على جزء من المعنى المراد بالجملة، وهو إما أن يستقل كل جزء من أجزائه بالدلالة أو لا يستقل فإن كان الأول فهو المركب التام، وهو إما أن يكون مركباً خبرياً أو ليس فإن كان مركباً خبرياً فلا يتصور تركبه من غير الأسماء كقولنا: زيد قائم أو من الأسماء والأفعال كقولنا: زيد قام أو
Chapter Two: On the Simple and the Compound. The simple expression is that which indicates, by designation, a meaning, and there is no indication of any part of it for anything at all, and it does not matter whether that meaning is simple like 'soul' and 'intellect' or compound like 'Zayd' and 'the animal', for a part of them, let it be the 'ya' and 'dal' of Zayd, and the 'ha' and 'ya' of 'al-hayawan', do not indicate anything by them. And when the 'ya' and 'dal' of Zayd or the 'ha' and 'ya' of 'al-hayawan' indicate something by them, they are not then a part of the word Zayd or 'al-hayawan'; because making some letters a part of others is not by virtue of the essences of the words, otherwise what is a part would be impossible not to be a part, and what is not a part would be impossible to become a part. Rather that depends on the positor's positioning and intention. And we know that the positor, when he made the 'ya' and 'dal' of Zayd a part of the word Zayd, did not intend by them indication of anything of Zayd's meaning nor of anything external to it. And when he made them indicative of something of Zayd or external to Zayd, he did not intend to make them a part of the word Zayd. And with this it becomes clear that there is no need for what is added in its definition from the perspective of being its part, for a thing cannot be conceived not to be a part when it is its part, nor when it is part of a part. And by this also appears the corruption of the opinion of one who specifies the non-indication of the part upon part of the meaning of the sentence, if that is due to his assumption that it indicates an external meaning in the state of being a part. And on this basis, 'Abdullah' and the like, when made a nickname and not intending its parts to indicate, in the state of composition, what they indicate in the state of isolation, then it is simple; otherwise it is compound. And the compound is that which has a part that indicates part of the meaning intended by the sentence. And it is either that each part of its parts is independent in indication or not independent. If the first, then it is the complete compound. And it is either a declarative compound or not. If it is a declarative compound, then it is inconceivable to be composed of other than nouns, like our saying: 'Zayd is standing', or from nouns and verbs like our saying: 'Zayd stood', or...
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage defines the fundamental distinction between simple (mufrad) and compound (murakkab) expressions, a key classification in logic because it determines the structure of propositions. The author begins by defining mufrad: it indicates a meaning by waḍʿ (conventional designation), and no part of it indicates anything. This holds whether the meaning is simple (e.g., 'soul', 'intellect') or composite (e.g., 'Zayd', 'the animal'). The letters of these words do not individually signify. The author then discusses a subtlety: if the letters of a word happen to have independent meanings in other contexts (e.g., 'yā' as a vocative particle), then in that word they are not considered parts because the wāḍiʿ (the one who coined the term) did not intend them to carry meaning. The author uses this to refute an opinion that restricts the non-indication of parts only to cases where the part does not indicate part of the sentence's meaning. Then he gives the example of 'Abdullah' used as a proper name (laqab): if the parts 'abd' and 'Allah' are not intended to bear their original meanings within the compound, the word is considered simple; otherwise it is compound. Finally, the author defines murakkab as having a part that indicates part of the meaning of the whole. He divides murakkab into tāmm (complete) and ghayr tāmm (incomplete): complete when each part independently indicates a meaning. He then subdivides tāmm into khabarī (declarative) and other types, with examples 'Zayd qā'im' and 'Zayd qāma'. The passage ends as he begins to discuss non-declarative compounds.

Key Terms
المركب
المركب التام
المركب الخبري

Key Takeaway
A complete compound is one where every part stands on its own in indicating meaning, which is typically the case with a sentence.

?

The Three Types of Single Word: Noun, Verb, and Particle
Author divides single word into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any single expression in Arabic as noun, verb, or particle using the Mantiq criteria of independence and need for a source or external time.

Core Idea
In logic, the category of a word depends on whether it can independently form part of a proposition.

In this passage, the author lays out the foundational division of single words (mufrad) into three categories: noun (ism), verb (kalima), and particle (adah). The key to classification is whether the word can serve as a standalone part of a predicative proposition (like subject or predicate) and whether it requires a source (masdar) for predication or implies an external time in its structure. This lesson will guide you through applying these criteria to any word.

Guided Path
1
Determine independence
Ask: Can this word alone be a subject or predicate of a proposition? If yes, it belongs to the first major division (noun or verb); if no, it is a particle.
Particles like 'min' or 'hal' cannot stand alone as a propositional limit.
2
Check need for a source
If it is independent, ask: Does it require a source (masdar) to be predicated? Nouns like 'Zayd' do not; verbs like 'yaqum' (he stands) do need a source (al-qiyam) for predication.
A source is an abstract verbal noun like 'standing'.
3
Examine time implication
Does the word's structure imply an external time? For verbs, the form implies a tense; for nouns, no external time is implied by the word itself.
Words like 'ams' (yesterday) have time built into their meaning, not the structure.
4
Classify
Combine the tests: independent + no source needed + no structural time = noun; independent + source needed + structural time = verb; not independent = particle.
Apply to examples like 'qalam', 'yaktubu', 'fi'.

الفصل الثالث في أقسام المفرد وهي الاسم والكلمة والأداة. نقول: اللفظ المفرد إما أن يدل على ما يصح استقلاله بجعله أحد جزأي القضية الخبرية أو ليس فإن كان الأول فهو إما أن يصح تركب القضية منه ولا تفتقر إلى مصدر يسند إليه ولا يلزمه لبنيته زمان خارج عن هويته لزوماً ذهنياً أو ليس فإن كان الأول فهو الاسم، وإن كان الثاني فهو الكلمة وأما ما هو قسيم القسم الأول فهو الأداة، وعند هذا فيجب أن يعرف كل واحد منهما على التفصيل وتقديم الاسم والكلمة على الأداة وإن كان واجباً نظراً إلى ما أشرنا إليه من الصفة الكمالية لكل واحد منهما، فتقديم الاسم على الكلمة أيضاً واجب بالنظر إلى هذا المعنى، إذ هو أشرف وأسمى، ولعل منشأ تسميته بالاسم إنما وقع مأخوذاً من هاهنا. والتحقيق في تميزه: إنما هو بأخذ ما أشرنا إليه من الخواص اللازمة له أو أخذ بعضها وذلك بأن يضاف إليه مع ما له أنه غير مفتقر إلى مصدر يستند إليه، ولا يلزمه لبنيته زمان خارج عن هويته، وأنه مما يصح تركب القضية الخبرية من حديه، وإن شئت اقتصرت في التعريف على واحدة منها، فإنه كافٍ على ما سنبينه، وهو كما في زيد وعمرو ونحوه، فإنه لا محالة غير مفتقر إلى مصدر يستند إليه وإن تصريفه ممتنع والقضية منتظمة من حديه كما في قولنا زيد قائم وعمرو قاعد ونحوه، وما يتخيل من ملازمة الزمان لمدلوله كما في أمس وغدا والزمان والصَّبوح والغبوق فالزمان داخل في مدلول لفظه وجزء من معناه لا أنه خارج لازم، وما هو خاصة الاسم إنما هو انتفاء ملازمة الزمان الخارج لمدلوله، وهي غير منتفية عنه في هذه الصور، وما يتخيل من ملازمة الزمان الخارجي لمدلولات بعض الأسماء كزيد وعمرو من ضرورة وجودها فيه فليس ذلك مما هو لازم لبنيته وإنما هو إن لزم فعن أمر خارج، حتى أنا لو قطعنا النظر عن كونه في الزمان لم يكن لازماً، وقد اضطربت آراء المنطقيين في أشياء مثل قولنا: غير بصير ولا جابر ولا عادل ونحو ذلك مما دخل عليه حرف السلب فقال قوم إنها ليست أسماء؛ إذ الاسم مفرد، وهي مركبة من اسم محصل وأداة. وقال قوم إنها أسماء، وسموها معدولة والواجب أنها حولت ألقاباً وأسماء علمية فليست مركبة، وإن قصد بها الدلالة على نفي البصر أو العدل أو الجور كانت مركبة ولا تكون اسماً ومن هذا القبيل ما تغير عن وضعه الأصلي للحوق لاحق من حركة، أو إعراب، كقولنا: زيد وعمرو بالضم فيهما، فإن الضمة اللاحقة بزيد غيرته عن وضعه الأصلي، حتى امتنع أن يلحق به عامل آخر مثل من وفي وعلى ونحوه، فإنه لا يمكنك أن تقول من زيد وعلى عمرو. ويذهب بعضهم إلى القول بنفي الاسمية عن هذا وأمثاله، وزعم أن الضمة اللاحقة بزيد دالة على معنى زيد على ما دل عليه لفظ زيد بالتسكين عند تجرده عنها، فكان مركباً، والاسم من شرطه أن يكون مفرداً. ومنهم من جعله اسماً وسماه مصرفاً. والحق أن زيداً مع الضمة أو غيرها من الحركات إن جعل لقباً بحيث لا يفهم المدلول دون المجموع فهو اسم، وإن قصد بالضمة معنى زائداً على مدلول اسم زيد كان مركباً، وإن قدّر عدم استقلال الضمة بالدلالة دون الاسم؛ إذ المركب إنما هو ما هو، لا من جهة استقلال أجزائه بالدلالة بل من جهة ما له جزء دال، وهو أعم من المستقل كما سلف. نعم، إن قدّر بالنظر إلى الوضع عدم وقوع الحروف السوالب والحركات أجزاءً من الأسماء فليس ذلك لاستحالته في نفسه، بل لو قدر وقوع ذلك من بعض الواضعين لما كان ممتنعاً. وأما الكلمة فمأخوذ من نقيض ما عرفنا به الاسم من الخواص، أما الكل
Chapter Three: On the divisions of the single word, which are noun, verb, and particle. We say: The single expression either indicates what is valid to be independent by being made one of the two limits of the predicative proposition, or not. If it is the first, then either it is valid for the proposition to be composed from it without needing a source to be predicated to it, nor does its structure necessitate a time external to its essence as a mental necessity, or not. If it is the first, then it is the noun, and if it is the second, then it is the verb. As for what is the counterpart of the first division, it is the particle. At this point, each of them must be known in detail, and the noun and verb are given precedence over the particle, though it is obligatory, in view of what we have indicated of the perfection attribute for each of them. Giving precedence to the noun over the verb is also obligatory in view of this meaning, because it is nobler and higher. Perhaps the origin of its naming as 'ism' is taken from here. The verification of its distinction is only by taking what we have indicated of the necessary properties for it, or taking some of them, that is, by adding to it with what it has: that it is not in need of a source to which it is predicated, nor does its structure necessitate a time external to its essence, and that it is valid for the predicative proposition to be composed from its two limits. If you wish, you may confine the definition to one of them, for it is sufficient, as we will explain. That is, as in 'Zayd' and 'Amr' and the like, for it is undoubtedly not in need of a source to which it is predicated, and its inflection is impossible, and the proposition is arranged from its two limits, as in our saying 'Zayd is standing' and 'Amr is sitting' and the like. And what is imagined of the necessity of time for its signification, as in 'yesterday', 'tomorrow', 'time', 'morning drink', and 'evening drink', the time is inside the signification of its expression and part of its meaning, not that it is external and necessary. What is the specific property of the noun is only the negation of the necessity of external time for its signification, and it is not negated from it in these forms. And what is imagined of the necessity of external time for the significations of some nouns, like 'Zayd' and 'Amr', due to the necessity of their existence in time, that is not something necessary for its structure; rather, if it is necessary, it is from an external matter, such that if we were to disregard its being in time, it would not be necessary. The opinions of the logicians have disagreed about things such as our saying 'non-seer', 'no-compeller', 'not just', and the like where the particle of negation has entered. Some said they are not nouns, for the noun is single, and they are composed of a positive noun and a particle. Others said they are nouns, and called them 'modified'. The correct view is that if they are turned into titles and proper names, then they are not composite; but if it is intended by them to indicate the negation of sight, or justice, or oppression, then they are composite and are not nouns. From this category is what changes from its original form due to a subsequent addition of a vowel or inflection, like our saying 'Zaydun' and 'Amrun' with the dammah on them. Indeed, the dammah attaching to Zayd changes it from its original form, to the extent that it prevents another operator like 'min', 'fi', and 'ala' from attaching to it, for you cannot say 'min Zaydin' and 'ala Amrin'. Some hold the opinion of denying nounhood to this and its likes, claiming that the dammah attaching to Zayd indicates a meaning of Zayd in addition to what the expression 'Zayd' indicated when it was still and stripped of it, so it is composite, and the condition of the noun is that it be single. Others have made it a noun and called it 'inflected'. The truth is that 'Zayd' with the dammah or other vowels: if it is made a title such that the signification is not understood without the whole, then it is a noun; but if the dammah is intended to give a meaning additional to the signification of the noun 'Zayd', then it is composite. Even if it is supposed that the dammah does not independently indicate without the noun, for the composite is only what has a part that indicates, not from the perspective of the independence of its parts in indication, but from the perspective of its having a part that indicates, and that is more general than the independent, as has passed. Yes, if it is supposed with regard to the original convention that negative letters and vowels do not occur as parts of nouns, that is not because it is impossible in itself; rather, if such occurrence were supposed from some classifiers, it would not be impossible. As for the verb, it is taken from the opposite of the properties by which we defined the noun.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition of the three types of single word (al-mufrad) in logic: noun (ism), verb (kalima), and particle (adah). The author begins by stating the division: an expression is either an independent part of a predicative proposition (like a subject or predicate) or not. If it is independent, it either does not need an external source (masdar) for predication and does not imply external time—this is the noun; or it does need those—this is the verb. Non-independent expressions are particles. The author then explains that the noun takes precedence due to its perfection. To precisely distinguish the noun, one may use any one of its necessary properties: not requiring a source for predication, not implying external time in its structure, and being suitable as a limit of a proposition. Examples are given like 'Zayd' and 'Amr'. The author addresses misunderstandings: words like 'yesterday' or 'tomorrow' seem to involve time, but that time is part of their meaning, not an external necessity. For proper names like 'Zayd', any temporal contingency is from external existence, not from the word's structure. The discussion then moves to problematic cases: expressions with a negative prefix (ghayr basir, etc.). Some logicians deny they are nouns because they are composite; others accept them as 'modified' (ma'dula). The author's position: if used as proper titles, they are nouns; if used to negate, they are composite. Similarly, inflected forms like 'Zaydun' (with dammah) are debated: some say the vowel adds meaning making it composite; others say it is still a noun called 'musarraf'. The truth depends on intention: if the vowel is part of the title, it is a noun; if it adds independent meaning, it is composite. The author clarifies that such composition is not impossible in itself, but by convention letters of negation and vowels are not parts of nouns. Finally, the verb is defined as the opposite of the noun's properties, though the passage cuts off mid-sentence.

Key Terms
الاسم
الكلمة
الأداة
المفرد

Key Takeaway
A noun is an independent expression that does not require a source for predication and whose structure does not necessitate an external time.

Understanding the Time Property of Nouns
Author explains time property of nouns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between external time (from existence) and internal time (necessitated by the word's structure) and apply this distinction to classify time-related words as nouns.

Core Idea
Time words like 'yesterday' have time inside their meaning, not externally imposed by their structure.

One of the most subtle aspects of defining the noun is the issue of time. Some words, like 'yesterday' (ams) or 'time' (zaman), clearly involve temporality, which might lead one to think they are verbs. The author clarifies that such words actually have time as part of their meaning, not as a structural necessity. In contrast, the time associated with a person like Zayd's existence is external to the word 'Zayd'. This lesson will walk you through this distinction to avoid misclassification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word's basic meaning
Ask: Does the word primarily signify a concept that inherently involves time (like a time period or an event)?
Words like 'yesterday' and 'time' clearly refer to temporal concepts.
2
Determine if time is in the meaning or structure
If the time is part of the word's meaning (i.e., if you understand the word, you understand time), then it is internal. If the word's form itself indicates a tense (like verb conjugations), then it is structural.
For 'yesterday', the time 'past day' is its meaning; for 'writes', the present tense is structural.
3
Apply the noun's property
The noun property is 'negation of necessity of external time'. Since time is internal to the meaning, it is not external, so the word is still a noun.
External time would be a time that is not part of the word's signification but arises from the word's structure.
4
Distinguish existential time
For proper names like 'Zayd', any time association (e.g., Zayd exists at some time) comes from the external existence of the person, not from the word itself. Therefore, it does not violate the noun property.
The author says: 'if we were to disregard its being in time, it would not be necessary.'

Source Text

ضرورة وجودها فيه فليس ذلك مما هو لازم لبنيته وإنما هو إن لزم فعن أمر خارج، حتى أنا لو قطعنا النظر عن كونه في الزمان لم يكن لازماً، وقد اضطربت آراء المنطقيين في أشياء مثل قولنا: غير بصير ولا جابر ولا قصد بها الدلالة على نفي البصر أو العدل أو الجور كانت مركبة ولا تكون اسماً ومن هذا القبيل ما تغير عن وضعه الأصلي للحوق لاحق من حركة، أو إعراب، كقولنا: زيد وعمرو بالضم فيهما، فإن الضمة اللاحقة بزيد غيرته عن وضعه الأصلي، حتى امتنع أن يلحق به عامل آخر مثل من وفي وعلى ونحوه، فإنه لا يمكنك أن تقول من زيد وعلى عمرو. ويذهب بعضهم إلى القول بنفي الاسمية عن هذا وأمثاله، وزعم أن الضمة اللاحقة بزيد دالة على معنى زيد على ما دل عليه لفظ زيد بالتسكين عند تجرده عنها، فكان مركباً، والاسم من شرطه أن يكون مفرداً. ومنهم من جعله اسماً وسماه مصرفاً. والحق أن زيداً مع الضمة أو غيرها من الحركات إن جعل لقباً بحيث لا يفهم المدلول دون المجموع فهو اسم، وإن قصد بالضمة معنى زائداً على مدلول اسم زيد كان مركباً، وإن قدّر عدم استقلال الضمة بالدلالة دون الاسم؛ إذ المركب إنما هو ما هو، لا من جهة استقلال أجزائه بالدلالة بل من جهة ما له جزء دال، وهو أعم من المستقل كما سلف. نعم، إن قدّر بالنظر إلى الوضع عدم وقوع الحروف السوالب والحركات أجزاءً من الأسماء فليس ذلك لاستحالته في نفسه، بل لو قدر وقوع ذلك من بعض الواضعين لما كان ممتنعاً. وأما الكلمة فمأخوذ من نقيض ما عرفنا به الاسم من الخواص، أما الكل
Chapter Three: On the divisions of the single word, which are noun, verb, and particle. We say: The single expression either indicates what is valid to be independent by being made one of the two limits of the predicative proposition, or not. If it is the first, then either it is valid for the proposition to be composed from it without needing a source to be predicated to it, nor does its structure necessitate a time external to its essence as a mental necessity, or not. If it is the first, then it is the noun, and if it is the second, then it is the verb. As for what is the counterpart of the first division, it is the particle. At this point, each of them must be known in detail, and the noun and verb are given precedence over the particle, though it is obligatory, in view of what we have indicated of the perfection attribute for each of them. Giving precedence to the noun over the verb is also obligatory in view of this meaning, because it is nobler and higher. Perhaps the origin of its naming as 'ism' is taken from here. The verification of its distinction is only by taking what we have indicated of the necessary properties for it, or taking some of them, that is, by adding to it with what it has: that it is not in need of a source to which it is predicated, nor does its structure necessitate a time external to its essence, and that it is valid for the predicative proposition to be composed from its two limits. If you wish, you may confine the definition to one of them, for it is sufficient, as we will explain. That is, as in 'Zayd' and 'Amr' and the like, for it is undoubtedly not in need of a source to which it is predicated, and its inflection is impossible, and the proposition is arranged from its two limits, as in our saying 'Zayd is standing' and 'Amr is sitting' and the like. And what is imagined of the necessity of time for its signification, as in 'yesterday', 'tomorrow', 'time', 'morning drink', and 'evening drink', the time is inside the signification of its expression and part of its meaning, not that it is external and necessary. What is the specific property of the noun is only the negation of the necessity of external time for its signification, and it is not negated from it in these forms. And what is imagined of the necessity of external time for the significations of some nouns, like 'Zayd' and 'Amr', due to the necessity of their existence in time, that is not something necessary for its structure; rather, if it is necessary, it is from an external matter, such that if we were to disregard its being in time, it would not be necessary. The opinions of the logicians have disagreed about things such as our saying 'non-seer', 'no-compeller', 'not just', and the like where the particle of negation has entered. Some said they are not nouns, for the noun is single, and they are composed of a positive noun and a particle. Others said they are nouns, and called them 'modified'. The correct view is that if they are turned into titles and proper names, then they are not composite; but if it is intended by them to indicate the negation of sight, or justice, or oppression, then they are composite and are not nouns. From this category is what changes from its original form due to a subsequent addition of a vowel or inflection, like our saying 'Zaydun' and 'Amrun' with the dammah on them. Indeed, the dammah attaching to Zayd changes it from its original form, to the extent that it prevents another operator like 'min', 'fi', and 'ala' from attaching to it, for you cannot say 'min Zaydin' and 'ala Amrin'. Some hold the opinion of denying nounhood to this and its likes, claiming that the dammah attaching to Zayd indicates a meaning of Zayd in addition to what the expression 'Zayd' indicated when it was still and stripped of it, so it is composite, and the condition of the noun is that it be single. Others have made it a noun and called it 'inflected'. The truth is that 'Zayd' with the dammah or other vowels: if it is made a title such that the signification is not understood without the whole, then it is a noun; but if the dammah is intended to give a meaning additional to the signification of the noun 'Zayd', then it is composite. Even if it is supposed that the dammah does not independently indicate without the noun, for the composite is only what has a part that indicates, not from the perspective of the independence of its parts in indication, but from the perspective of its having a part that indicates, and that is more general than the independent, as has passed. Yes, if it is supposed with regard to the original convention that negative letters and vowels do not occur as parts of nouns, that is not because it is impossible in itself; rather, if such occurrence were supposed from some classifiers, it would not be impossible. As for the verb, it is taken from the opposite of the properties by which we defined the noun.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition of the three types of single word (al-mufrad) in logic: noun (ism), verb (kalima), and particle (adah). The author begins by stating the division: an expression is either an independent part of a predicative proposition (like a subject or predicate) or not. If it is independent, it either does not need an external source (masdar) for predication and does not imply external time—this is the noun; or it does need those—this is the verb. Non-independent expressions are particles. The author then explains that the noun takes precedence due to its perfection. To precisely distinguish the noun, one may use any one of its necessary properties: not requiring a source for predication, not implying external time in its structure, and being suitable as a limit of a proposition. Examples are given like 'Zayd' and 'Amr'. The author addresses misunderstandings: words like 'yesterday' or 'tomorrow' seem to involve time, but that time is part of their meaning, not an external necessity. For proper names like 'Zayd', any temporal contingency is from external existence, not from the word's structure. The discussion then moves to problematic cases: expressions with a negative prefix (ghayr basir, etc.). Some logicians deny they are nouns because they are composite; others accept them as 'modified' (ma'dula). The author's position: if used as proper titles, they are nouns; if used to negate, they are composite. Similarly, inflected forms like 'Zaydun' (with dammah) are debated: some say the vowel adds meaning making it composite; others say it is still a noun called 'musarraf'. The truth depends on intention: if the vowel is part of the title, it is a noun; if it adds independent meaning, it is composite. The author clarifies that such composition is not impossible in itself, but by convention letters of negation and vowels are not parts of nouns. Finally, the verb is defined as the opposite of the noun's properties, though the passage cuts off mid-sentence.

Key Terms
الزمان
داخل في المدلول
خارج لازم

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is 'yesterday' (ams) still a noun despite implying time?
A) Because 'yesterday' does not actually involve time.
B) Because the time in 'yesterday' is internal to its meaning, not necessitated by its structure.
C) Because 'yesterday' is a verb in other languages.
D) Because the author says it is a particle.

Key Takeaway
Time in the meaning of a noun does not violate its definition; only structural external time does.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Problematic Cases: Modified Expressions and Inflected Nouns
Author discusses problematic cases for nounhood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether an expression like 'ghayr basir' or 'Zaydun' is a noun or composite based on its usage as a title or compositional meaning.

Core Idea
Expressions with negative particles or inflectional endings can be nouns if they function as proper names, not if they carry compositional meaning.

The definition of noun becomes complicated when we encounter expressions that are not simple single words. For example, 'ghayr basir' (non-seer) contains a negation particle, and 'Zaydun' (with dammah) includes a vowel that might convey additional meaning. The author examines varying opinions among logicians and provides his own criterion: if such expressions are used as proper titles (alqab), they are nouns; if they are used to convey a compositional meaning (like negating sight), they are composite and not nouns. This lesson will help you apply this criterion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the presence of additional elements
Look for negative particles (ghayr, la, etc.) or inflectional vowels (dammah, fathah) attached to the base word.
Examples: 'ghayr basir' (non-seer), 'Zaydun' (with dammah).
2
Determine the intended function
Ask: Is the expression used as a proper name or title (like a nickname) for a specific person, or is it used descriptively to convey a negative meaning?
If it is a title, the whole expression functions as a single noun; if descriptive, it is composite.
3
Check if the additional element carries independent meaning
If the vowel or particle independently signifies something (like negation), and the expression is used for that meaning, then it is composite. If the vowel is just part of the title without independent signification, it is a noun.
The author says: 'if the dammah is intended to give a meaning additional to the signification of the noun Zayd, then it is composite.'
4
Apply the rule to classify
Classify as noun if used as a title (like calling someone Ghayr Basir as a name), or composite if used descriptively. The same applies to inflected forms: 'Zaydun' as a title is a noun; if the dammah indicates grammatical function (like subject), it may be composite.
The author notes that by convention, such elements are not normally parts of nouns, but it is not impossible in itself.

الفصل الثالث في أقسام المفرد وهي الاسم والكلمة والأداة. نقول: اللفظ المفرد إما أن يدل على ما يصح استقلاله بجعله أحد جزأي القضية الخبرية أو ليس فإن كان الأول فهو إما أن يصح تركب القضية منه ولا تفتقر إلى مصدر يسند إليه ولا يلزمه لبنيته زمان خارج عن هويته لزوماً ذهنياً أو ليس فإن كان الأول فهو الاسم، وإن كان الثاني فهو الكلمة وأما ما هو قسيم القسم الأول فهو الأداة، وعند هذا فيجب أن يعرف كل واحد منهما على التفصيل وتقديم الاسم والكلمة على الأداة وإن كان واجباً نظراً إلى ما أشرنا إليه من الصفة الكمالية لكل واحد منهما، فتقديم الاسم على الكلمة أيضاً واجب بالنظر إلى هذا المعنى، إذ هو أشرف وأسمى، ولعل منشأ تسميته بالاسم إنما وقع مأخوذاً من هاهنا. والتحقيق في تميزه: إنما هو بأخذ ما أشرنا إليه من الخواص اللازمة له أو أخذ بعضها وذلك بأن يضاف إليه مع ما له أنه غير مفتقر إلى مصدر يستند إليه، ولا يلزمه لبنيته زمان خارج عن هويته، وأنه مما يصح تركب القضية الخبرية من حديه، وإن شئت اقتصرت في التعريف على واحدة منها، فإنه كافٍ على ما سنبينه، وهو كما في زيد وعمرو ونحوه، فإنه لا محالة غير مفتقر إلى مصدر يستند إليه وإن تصريفه ممتنع والقضية منتظمة من حديه كما في قولنا زيد قائم وعمرو قاعد ونحوه، وما يتخيل من ملازمة الزمان لمدلوله كما في أمس وغدا والزمان والصَّبوح والغبوق فالزمان داخل في مدلول لفظه وجزء من معناه لا أنه خارج لازم، وما هو خاصة الاسم إنما هو انتفاء ملازمة الزمان الخارج لمدلوله، وهي غير منتفية عنه في هذه الصور، وما يتخيل من ملازمة الزمان الخارجي لمدلولات بعض الأسماء كزيد وعمرو من ضرورة وجودها فيه فليس ذلك مما هو لازم لبنيته وإنما هو إن لزم فعن أمر خارج، حتى أنا لو قطعنا النظر عن كونه في الزمان لم يكن لازماً، وقد اضطربت آراء المنطقيين في أشياء مثل قولنا: غير بصير ولا جابر ولا عادل ونحو ذلك مما دخل عليه حرف السلب فقال قوم إنها ليست أسماء؛ إذ الاسم مفرد، وهي مركبة من اسم محصل وأداة. وقال قوم إنها أسماء، وسموها معدولة والواجب أنها حولت ألقاباً وأسماء علمية فليست مركبة، وإن قصد بها الدلالة على نفي البصر أو العدل أو الجور كانت مركبة ولا تكون اسماً ومن هذا القبيل ما تغير عن وضعه الأصلي للحوق لاحق من حركة، أو إعراب، كقولنا: زيد وعمرو بالضم فيهما، فإن الضمة اللاحقة بزيد غيرته عن وضعه الأصلي، حتى امتنع أن يلحق به عامل آخر مثل من وفي وعلى ونحوه، فإنه لا يمكنك أن تقول من زيد وعلى عمرو. ويذهب بعضهم إلى القول بنفي الاسمية عن هذا وأمثاله، وزعم أن الضمة اللاحقة بزيد دالة على معنى زيد على ما دل عليه لفظ زيد بالتسكين عند تجرده عنها، فكان مركباً، والاسم من شرطه أن يكون مفرداً. ومنهم من جعله اسماً وسماه مصرفاً. والحق أن زيداً مع الضمة أو غيرها من الحركات إن جعل لقباً بحيث Privative Terms: Simple Names or Compounds?
Author resolves debate on privative terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a privative term (معدولة) such as غير بصير is a simple name or compound according to the criteria given in the passage.

Core Idea
A privative term is a name when used as a title; compound when negating a quality.

The status of privative terms like غير بصير has been debated among logicians. One group argues they are compound because they consist of a positive name and a negative particle; another treats them as names. The author resolves the debate by distinguishing between use as a proper title (لقب) and use as a description that negates a quality. This lesson will help you apply that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing views
One view denies namehood because of the negative particle; the other grants namehood and calls them معدولة.
The particle of negation is حرف السلب.
2
Extract the author's first condition
If the privative expression is used as a proper title (لقب), it is a simple name.
Example: غير بصير as a name for a person.
3
Extract the author's second condition
If it is used to convey the negation of the quality, it is compound and not a name.
Example: غير بصير meaning 'not seeing'.
4
Apply the condition to an example
Given غير بصير in context, decide whether it is a name or compound based on whether it is a title or a description.
Context is key.

وقد اضطربت آراء المنطقيين في أشياء مثل قولنا : (غير بصير) ولا جابر) و لا عادل ونحو ذلك مما دخل عليه حرف السلب فقال قوم إنها ليست أسماء؛ إذ الاسم مفرد، وهي مركبة من اسم محصل وأداة. وقال قوم إنها أسماء، وسموها (معدولة) والواجب أنها حولت ألقاباً وأسماء علمية فليست مركبة، وإن قصد بها الدلالة على نفي البصر أو العدل أو الجور كانت مركبة ولا تكون اسماً ومن هذا القبيل ما تغير عن وضعه الأصلي للحوق لاحق من حركة، أو إعراب، كقولنا: زيد وعمرو) بالضم فيهما، فإن الضمة اللاحقة بزيد غيَّرته عن وضعه الأصلي، حتى امتنع أن يلحق به عامل آخر مثل (من) و (في) و (على) ونحوه، فإنه لا يمكنك أن تقول من زيد وعلى عمرو). ويذهب بعضهم إلى القول بنفي الاسمية عن هذا وأمثاله، وزعم أن الضمة اللاحقة بزيد دالة على معنى زيد على ما دل عليه لفظ زيد بالتسكين عند تجرده عنها، فكان مركباً ، والاسم من شرطه أن يكون مفرداً. ومنهم من جعله اسماً وسماه مصرفاً. والحق أن زيداً مع الضمة أو غيرها من الحركات إن جعل لقباً بحيث لا يفهم المدلول دون المجموع فهو اسم، وإن قصد بالضمة معنى زائداً على مدلول اسم زيد كان مركباً، وإن قدّر عدم استقلال الضمة بالدلالة دون الاسم؛ إذ المركب إنما هو ما هو ، لا من جهة استقلال أجزائه بالدلالة بل من جهة ما له جزء دال، وهو أعم من المستقل كما سلف. نعم، إن قدّر بالنظر إلى الوضع عدم وقوع الحروف السوالب والحركات أجزاءً من الأسماء فليس ذلك لاستحالته في نفسه، بل لو قدر وقوع ذلك من بعض الواضعين لما كان ممتنعاً.
And the opinions of the logicians have been confused regarding things like our saying: 'non-seeing', 'non-compeller', 'non-just', and the like, which the particle of negation enters upon. Some said they are not names, because the name is singular, and they are composed of a positive name and a tool. Others said they are names, and called them 'equated'. And the required view is that they have been turned into titles and proper names, so they are not compound. But if by them is intended the negation of sight, justice, or injustice, they are compound and are not a name. And of this type is what changes from its original form due to a subsequent change of movement or inflection, like our saying: 'Zayd' and 'Amr' with dammah on both, for the dammah attached to Zayd changes it from its original form, so that another operator like 'min', 'fi', 'ala', and the like cannot attach to it, so you cannot say 'min Zayd' and 'ala Amr'. And some go to the extent of denying namehood to this and its likes, claiming that the dammah attached to Zayd indicates a meaning for Zayd in addition to what the word Zayd with sukun indicates when stripped of it, so it would be compound, and the name is conditioned to be singular. And among them is he who made it a name and called it 'inflected'. And the truth is that Zayd with dammah or other movements, if made a title such that the referent is not understood without the whole, it is a name. And if by the dammah an extra meaning beyond the meaning of the name Zayd is intended, it is compound. Even if the non-independence of the dammah in indication without the name is assumed, the compound is what it is, not due to the independence of its parts in indication but due to its having a part that indicates, and that is more general than the independent, as previously mentioned. Yes, if one assumes, with respect to convention, that negative letters and movements do not occur as parts of names, that is not due to its impossibility in itself, but if such occurrence from some coiners were assumed, it would not be impossible.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage addresses two cases that challenge the definition of 'ism' (name) as a singular word. First, privative terms like 'غير بصير' (non-seeing) which include a negative particle. Some logicians argue they are compound because they consist of a name and a particle; others treat them as simple names called 'ma'dulah'. The author resolves that when such terms are used as proper titles (ألقاب), they are simple names; but if they are intended to express negation of a quality, they are compound. Second, inflected terms like 'زيد' with a case ending (e.g., dammah). The inflection changes the word from its base form and prevents other operators from attaching. The author presents the conflicting views: some deny namehood because the inflection adds meaning; others accept it as a 'musarraf' (inflected name). The correct view, according to the author, is that if the inflected form is used as a conventional title where the inflection does not add independent meaning, it is a name. However, if the inflection is intended to convey an additional grammatical meaning (like case), it becomes compound. The author emphasizes that compoundness does not require independent parts but only that a part indicates something. Finally, the author notes that the exclusion of negative particles and inflections from being parts of names is a matter of convention, not logical necessity.

Key Terms
معدولة
اسم
مركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is a privative expression like غير بصير a simple name?
A) When it is used to negate the quality of sight.
B) When it has become a proper title (لقب) such that the referent is not understood without the whole.
C) Always, because it is a single word in Arabic.
D) Never, it is always compound due to the negative particle.

Key Takeaway
The classification of privative terms as names or compounds depends on their usage: as a proper title they are names; as a description negating a quality they are compounds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the phrase غير مستقيم (non-straight) in a text, determine whether it should be considered a single name or a compound based on the author's criteria. What additional context would you need?
?
Provide a real Islamic example where a privative term is used as a proper title (e.g., a name of a person or a tribe) and explain why it would be a name.

Inflected Terms: Names or Compounds?
Author resolves debate on inflected terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether an inflected noun like زيد with dammah is a simple name or compound according to the author's resolution.

Core Idea
An inflected noun is a name if the inflection is part of the conventional title; compound if the inflection adds independent meaning.

Inflected nouns such as زيد with a case ending challenge the definition of 'name'. The inflection changes the word and may seem to add meaning. Logicians differ; the author provides a nuanced solution: if the inflected form is a conventional title, it is a name; if the inflection conveys an additional grammatical meaning, it is compound. This lesson will help you apply that criterion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
The word زيد with dammah is different from زيد with sukun; the dammah prevents other operators from attaching.
Example: you cannot say 'من زيد' because the dammah changes the form.
2
Recognize the two opposing views
Some deny namehood because the dammah adds meaning; others accept it as 'musarraf' (inflected name).
3
Extract the author's condition
If the inflected form is used as a conventional title (لقب) where the whole indicates a single referent, it is a name. If the inflection is intended to convey an extra meaning (like case), it is compound.
The author emphasizes that compoundness does not require independent parts; it suffices that a part indicates something.
4
Apply the condition to a new example
Consider an inflected noun in context and decide whether it is a name or compound based on the intention.
Context is crucial.

The Logical Definition of 'Kalimah' (Verb)
Author defines logical kalimah by contrast with ism and states its properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three essential characteristics of a logical kalimah and explain how it differs from the grammatical fi'l.

Core Idea
A logical kalimah must indicate a meaning with time, be derived from a masdar, and refer to an unspecified subject.

In this passage, the author defines the logical term 'kalimah' by contrasting it with 'ism' (noun). Unlike grammar, logic requires that a verb include a temporal dimension and not specify its subject independently. This distinction is fundamental for understanding how propositions are formed and evaluated in logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Extract the three defining properties
From the opening lines, identify that the kalimah: (1) carries a qualified time (al-zaman al-muhassal), (2) is taken from a masdar (verbal noun), and (3) the author also later mentions an unspecified subject. Note how these properties distinguish it from nouns.
The property of unspecified subject is explicitly stated later in the formal definition.
2
Compare with the grammatical fi'l
The author states that what logicians call 'kalimah' is called 'fi'l' by grammarians, and all that grammarians call 'fi'l' is a kalimah. Understand that the logical category encompasses all grammatical verbs, but not all logical kalimah may be recognized as fi'l in grammar? Actually the passage suggests they are coextensive in reference.
Be careful: 'kalimah' in logic is a technical term, not the general word.
3
Examine the temporal condition
The kalimah must indicate one of the three times (past, present, future). This is what 'qualified time' means. For example, 'qama' indicates past, 'yaqumu' indicates present or future. This temporality is essential and lacking in nouns.
The author says 'ملازمة الزمان المحصل لبنيته' - the accompaniment of time is in its structure.
4
Apply the definition to examples
Test whether 'qama' (he stood) fits: it has time (past), is derived from qiyam, and subject is unspecified (who stood? not specified by the word). So it is a kalimah. Compare with 'Zaydun' (a noun) - no time, not derived from a masdar, not a kalimah.
The definition also requires singularity, but that is handled in subsequent lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلمة فمأخوذ من نقيض ما عرَّفنا به الاسم من الخواص، أما الكل جزوه فقد لا لا يخفى ملازمة الزمان المحصل لبنيته، وأخذه من المصدر الذي هو اسم الفعل واشتقاقه منه، ومنه يقال قام يقوم قياماً) و(قعد يقعد قعوداً) ولربما تشكل على الكلمة بملازمة الزمان لما ليس بكلمة كما أشرنا إليه وقد سبق وجه حله، وما سُمي كلمة في اصطلاح المنطقيين فيسمى في اصطلاح النحاة (فعلاً) ولا ينعكس كنفسه، حتى إن كل ما يسمى (فعلاً) فهو كلمة، فإنه وإن صح ذلك للأفعال في ما كان منها ماضياً مثل قولنا : قام وقعد لعدم استقلال جزء منه بالدلالة على شيء، حيث جعل يصح ذلك لها في ما كان مستقلاً كقولنا: (سوف يقوم) و(سيقوم) إذ السين وسوف من دالة على الاستقبال وتقدم على الفعل والمقصود من كل واحد حالة الاجتماع هو المقصود منه حالة الانفراد بخلاف ما في (الياء) و(الدال) من زيد و (الحاء) و(الياء) من الحيوان فلا تكون كلمته مفرداً، بل ما وقع من الأفعال في أوله إحدى الزوائد الأربع هي (التاء) من (تقوم) و(الياء) من (يقوم) و(النون) من (نقوم) و(الهمزة) من (أقوم)، وإن خلا عن دخول ما يخلصه للاستقبال فإنه أيضاً لا يكون كلمته، وسواء استعمل للحال أو للاستقبال وذلك أن (التاء) من (تمشي) تدل على المخاطب و الهمزة) من (أمشي) تدل على المتكلم وذلك ليس من الفعل في شيء وكل ما لجزئه دلالة على شيء ما ،فمركب وليس مفردا، والكلمة يجب أن تكون مفردة وعدم استقلال (الميم) و(الشين) و(الياء) بالدلالة من يمشي) و(أمشي) و (نمشي على الفعل إلا بإحدى الزوائد مما لا يمنع من التركيب كما ظن قوم، إذ ليس المركب ما أجزائه مستقلة بالدلالة، بل ما يوجد لأجزائه دلالات مختلفة كان كل واحد منها مستقلاً أولاً كما سبق تحقيقه، لكن من الحذاق من فرّق بين (الياء) من (يقوم) وبين غيرها من الحروف الزوائد حتى جعل (يقوم) ملحقاً فالكلمات من حيث إنها لا تدل إلا على موضوع غير معين وهو مما يتعدد وقوع التصديق به والتكذيب إلا بعد التصريح به بخلاف أخواتها، ولهذا قال في رسم الكلمة إنها اللفظ الدال على معنى لموضوع غير معين وزمان من الأزمنة الثلاثة، ولم يعلم أن التركيب في اللفظ إنما هو بسبب دلالة جزئه على شيء من معنى الجملة كان ذلك
As for 'kalimah', it is taken from the opposite of the properties by which we defined the name. As for the whole, its part may not be hidden: the accompaniment of qualified time in its structure, and its taking from the source which is the name of the action and its derivation from it. From it one says 'qama yaqumu qiyaman' and 'qa'ada yaq'udu qu'udan'. And sometimes difficulty arises for 'kalimah' due to the accompaniment of time for that which is not a 'kalimah', as we indicated, and its solution has preceded. And what is called 'kalimah' in the terminology of logicians is called 'fi'l' in the terminology of grammarians, but not vice versa, such that everything called 'fi'l' is a 'kalimah'. For although that holds for verbs that are past, like our saying 'qama' and 'qa'ada', because of the non-independence of any part of indicating anything, it is also valid for those that are independent, like our saying 'sawfa yaqumu' and 'sayaqumu', for the letters sin and sawfa indicate futurity and precede the verb, and the intended meaning from each in the state of combination is the intended meaning in the state of isolation, unlike the case of 'ya' and 'dal' in Zayd and 'ha' and 'ya' in hayawan, so they are not a singular kalimah. Rather, what occurs from verbs with one of the four prefixes in its beginning — which are 'ta' from 'taqumu', 'ya' from 'yaqumu', 'nun' from 'naqumu', 'hamza' from 'aqumu' — even if devoid of entering what specifies it for futurity, it also is not a kalimah, whether used for present or future. That is because the 'ta' from 'tamshi' indicates the addressee, and the 'hamza' from 'amshi' indicates the speaker, and that is not part of the verb at all. And everything whose part has an indication of something is compound and not singular, and the kalimah must be singular. And the non-independence of 'mim', 'shin', 'ya' in indication from 'yamshi', 'amshi', 'namshi' of the verb except with one of the prefixes does not prevent composition, as some people thought, because the compound is not that whose parts are independent in indication, but that which has various indications for its parts, whether each one is independent or not, as previously verified. However, some discerning scholars differentiated between the 'ya' of 'yaqumu' and other prefix letters, even making 'yaqumu' annexed. For the kalimah, in that it does not indicate except an unspecified subject — and it is something that is subject to multiple instances of affirmation and denial except after specification — differs from its counterparts. Therefore, in the definition of kalimah it is said: it is the utterance indicating a meaning for an unspecified subject and one of the three times. And it is not known that composition in the utterance is only due to the indication of its part on something of the sentence meaning, whether that be
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage defines the logical term 'kalimah' (verb) by contrasting it with 'ism' (name). The author explains that kalimah is characterized by being derived from a masdar (verbal noun) and necessarily includes temporal meaning. The logical kalimah corresponds to what grammarians call 'fi'l', but the correspondence is not one-to-one because logical kalimah requires singularity. The author then addresses problematic cases: prefixed verbs like 'taqumu' (with person/gender markers) might seem compound because the prefix indicates the subject. The author argues that these are still kalimah because the prefix's indication is not independent of the whole; the meaning in combination is the same as conceptually united. The author also discusses the future particles 'sin' and 'sawfa', noting they do not make the expression compound because they do not add a separable meaning to the word. The passage clarifies that composition depends on whether a part indicates a separate aspect of the sentence meaning. The author concludes with the formal definition of kalimah from earlier: a word indicating a meaning with an unspecified subject and one of the three tenses. The closing sentence notes that composition in an utterance is due to a part indicating something of the sentence meaning.

Key Terms
الكلمة
المصدر
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an essential characteristic of the logical kalimah?
A) It can be used in any position in a sentence.
B) It is derived from a masdar and indicates a time.
C) It always includes a prefixed pronoun.
D) It must have a specified subject.

Key Takeaway
The logical kalimah is an utterance that indicates a meaning with a time and is derived from a masdar, and it corresponds to the grammatical verb but with additional conditions for singularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the verb 'na'budu' (we worship) from Surah Al-Fatihah. Using the three properties of kalimah, determine whether it is a logical kalimah. Then consider: does it have an unspecified subject? The prefix 'na' indicates the speaker (we). Is that a specification of the subject? According to the author, does this prevent it from being a kalimah? Hint: The author later argues that prefixed verbs remain singular because the prefix's indication is not independent.

Or vice versa? The passage says all fi'l are kalimah, but is there any exception? Consider the future particle + verb construction 'sawfa yaqumu' - grammarians may treat it as a verb phrase, but logicians consider it a single kalimah. Explain why using the passage's criteria.

Why 'Sawfa Yaqumu' is a Single Kalimah
Author explains why future particles do not make the verb compound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a verb preceded by 'sin' or 'sawfa' is considered a single logical kalimah rather than a compound expression.

Core Idea
Future particles do not make a verb compound because their meaning is inherently part of the temporal indication, not an independent subject or separate attribute.

The author anticipates an objection: if we add 'sawfa' to 'yaqumu', don't we have two words? The author explains that since 'sawfa' only adds futurity, which is already a temporal dimension of the verb, the combination does not introduce a separate meaning; the intended meaning of each part in isolation is the same as when combined. Hence, it remains a singular kalimah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problematic expression
The example 'sawfa yaqumu' (he will stand). In grammar, this is a particle plus a verb, but in logic, we ask: is it one word or two?
2
Apply the composition criterion
The author says a word is compound if any part independently indicates something. Check: Does 'sawfa' independently indicate futurity? Yes, it does, even in isolation. So one might think it is compound.
3
Understand the author's reasoning
The author argues that the intended meaning of 'sawfa' in isolation (futurity) is exactly the same as when it precedes the verb. And the verb 'yaqumu' independently indicates an action with time (present/future). When combined, they do not produce a new meaning beyond the combination of these two aspects. Crucially, the futurity indicated by 'sawfa' is not a separate part of the propositional content; it modifies the verb's time. Therefore, the expression is not compound because no part indicates something that is not also part of the whole's meaning. The key distinction: the prefix in 'yaqumu' (the ya) indicates the subject, which becomes a separate element, whereas future particles only add time.
The author contrasts this with prefixed verbs like 'taqumu' where the prefix indicates the addressee, making it compound.
4
Compare with prefixed verbs
Contrast with 'taqumu' where the 'ta' indicates the addressee, which is a separate component (subject). The author suggests that such prefixes do create composition because they introduce a different indication.
This highlights why future particles are different: they do not add a new subject or object, only time.

As for 'kalimah', it is taken from the opposite of the properties by which we defined the name. As for the whole, its part may not be hidden: the accompaniment of qualified time in its structure, and its taking from the source which is the name of the action and its derivation from it. From it one says 'qama yaqumu qiyaman' and 'qa'ada yaq'udu qu'udan'. And sometimes difficulty arises for 'kalimah' due to the accompaniment of time for that which is not a 'kalimah', as we indicated, and its solution has preceded. And what is called 'kalimah' in the terminology of logicians is called 'fi'l' in the terminology of grammarians, but not vice versa, such that everything called 'fi'l' is a 'kalimah'. For although that holds for verbs that are past, like our saying 'qama' and 'qa'ada', because of the non-independence of any part of indicating anything, it is also valid for those that are independent, like our saying 'sawfa yaqumu' and 'sayaqumu', for the letters sin and sawfa indicate futurity and precede the verb, and the intended meaning from each in the state of combination is the intended meaning in the state of isolation, unlike the case of 'ya' and 'dal' in Zayd and 'ha' and 'ya' in hayawan, so they are not a singular kalimah.
