That is because the 'ta' from 'tamshi' indicates the addressee, and the 'hamza' from 'amshi' indicates the speaker, and that is not part of the verb at all. And everything whose part has an indication of something is compound and not singular, and the kalimah must be singular. And the non-independence of 'mim', 'shin', 'ya' in indication from 'yamshi', 'amshi', 'namshi' of the verb except with one of the prefixes does not prevent composition, as some people thought, because the compound is not that whose parts are independent in indication, but that which has various indications for its parts, whether each one is independent or not, as previously verified. However, some discerning scholars differentiated between the 'ya' of 'yaqumu' and other prefix letters, even making 'yaqumu' annexed. For the kalimah, in that it does not indicate except an unspecified subject — and it is something that is subject to multiple instances of affirmation and denial except after specification — differs from its counterparts. Therefore, in the definition of kalimah it is said: it is the utterance indicating a meaning for an unspecified subject and one of the three times. Commentary
This passage defines the logical term 'kalimah' (verb) by contrasting it with 'ism' (name). The author explains that kalimah is characterized by being derived from a masdar (verbal noun) and necessarily includes temporal meaning. The logical kalimah corresponds to what grammarians call 'fi'l', but the correspondence is not one-to-one because logical kalimah requires singularity. The author then addresses problematic cases: prefixed verbs like 'taqumu' (with person/gender markers) might seem compound because the prefix indicates the subject. The author argues that these are still kalimah because the prefix's indication is not independent of the whole; the meaning in combination is the same as conceptually united. The author also discusses the future particles 'sin' and 'sawfa', noting they do not make the expression compound because they do not add a separable meaning to the word. The passage clarifies that composition depends on whether a part indicates a separate aspect of the sentence meaning. The author concludes with the formal definition of kalimah from earlier: a word indicating a meaning with an unspecified subject and one of the three tenses. The closing sentence notes that composition in an utterance is due to a part indicating something of the sentence meaning.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is 'sawfa yaqumu' considered a single kalimah?
A) Because 'sawfa' has no meaning.
B) Because the intended meaning of 'sawfa' in combination is the same as in isolation, and it does not add a separate subject.
C) Because grammarians say it is one word.
D) Because 'yaqumu' alone is not a kalimah.

Key Takeaway
The addition of a future particle does not make a verb compound because its indication is purely temporal and integrates with the verb's meaning, unlike prefixes that indicate the subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the Quranic verse: 'وَسَيَعْلَمُ الَّذِينَ ظَلَمُوا أَيَّ مُنقَلَبٍ يَنقَلِبُونَ' (And those who do wrong will come to know by what overturning they will be overturned). Divisions of Singular Words: Ism, Kalima, Ada
Author defines ism, kalima, ada and resolves objections about incomplete nouns, pronouns, existential verbs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any singular word as ism, kalima, or ada by applying the criteria of propositional independence, temporal indication, and meaning-in-itself.

Core Idea
A particle (ada) has no meaning in itself; it only indicates a meaning in another word.

In this lesson, we explore the logical division of singular words into nouns (ism), verbs (kalima), and particles (ada). The author defines each category and addresses potential objections by clarifying how incomplete nouns, pronouns, and existential verbs differ from particles. By the end, you will be able to apply these criteria to correctly classify any singular word in a proposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories and their definitions.
Read the passage carefully and note how the author defines ism as a word that can be subject or predicate, kalima as a verb requiring time and attribution to an infinitive, and ada as a word that cannot be part of a proposition and indicates a meaning in another word.
2
Understand the defining criterion for ada (particle).
The author emphasizes that a particle has no meaning in itself; it only serves to indicate a relation between other words. This is why it cannot serve as subject or predicate. Examine the examples 'min', 'fi', 'ala'.
3
Apply the criterion to the three objections.
The passage addresses three potential counterexamples: incomplete nouns (alladhi), pronouns (huwa), and existential verbs (kana). For each, explain why they are not particles: incomplete nouns become subject after specification; pronouns refer to a specific entity; existential verbs can function as full predicates when asserting existence.
This step involves resolving the apparent similarity to particles.

الفصل الثالث: في أقسام المفرد (عمرو) فإنه لو بدل أمرهما بالأداة لتبطل معنى القضية، وهو كما قيل زيد في أو على) حتى يقول: (زيد في الدار) أو ( على زيد درهم) وأما الأداة فلفظ مفرد لا يستقل بجعله أحد جزأي القضية لا مبتدأ ولا خبراً، وهي في الحقيقة لا تستعمل لمعنى في نفسها، بل لمعنى في غيرها، لذا سُمّيت أداة وهي كقولنا: (من) وفي وعلى ونحوها. وإن استشكل على ما أشرنا إليه في الأداة بالأسماء النواقص، والمضمرات، والكلمات الوجودية، من جهة تعذر استقلالها بما تستقل به الأسماء التامة والكلمات غير الوجودية. فالوجه في حله أن يقال: أما الأسماء النواقص كـ (الذي ونحوها فإنه إن امتنع أحد جزأي القضية حتى إنه لا يقال الذي (قائم) أو زيـد الـذي) فليس إلا لتقدر الإسناد إلى المبهم ، وإسناده وهو وإن تعذر ذلك فيه قبل التعيين بالصلة والعائد فلا يتعذر بعده، وهو أن يقال الذي هو أبيض (قائم) فالذي هو مبتدأ في القضية وأبيض صفته، ولا يخفى أن مثل هذا فمتعدد في الأداة. وأما المضمرات كـ هو وهي) ونحوه فإنه وإن تعذر جعلها من القضية الكائنة لا يقال: (هو قائم بل زيد (قائم) فليس إلا لما أشرنا إليه في الناقص، وإلا فمدلول (هو) وهي) في قولنا : زيـد هـو قـائـم) أو (زينب هي (قائمة ليس إلا نفس زيد وزينب، ولا كذلك الأدوات، وأما الكلمات الوجودية كـ (وجد، ويوجد، وكان، ويكون وكائن فإنها وإن امتنع فيها ذلك أيضاً فليس إلا إذا قصد بها ربط الموضوع بالمحمول كقولنا : زيد) وجد، أو يوجد، أو كان، أو يكون في العالم أو البلد وإلا فإن كان القصد بها ليس هو غير الإخبار عنه بوجوده وكونه مع كون ما أسند إليه من الأسماء التامة فقد تم الخبر، وما مثل هذا فمتعذر في الأداة، فعلى هذا مفارقة الاسم للكلمة والأداة في جواز تركب القضية الخبرية منه كقولنا: زيد) (قائم وتعذره في الكلمة والأداة، فإن ذلك مما لا ينتظم منهما كما قيل قام (قعد) (من) في على وما به مفارقة الكلمة للاسم والأداة فلزوم إسنادها إلى المصدر، ولزوم الزمان الخارجي لبنيتها، وما به مفارقة الأداة للاسم والكلمة فتعذر استقلالها بكونها أحد جزأي القضية، وإن استعمالها لا يكون لمعنى مقصود في نفسها، بل في غيرها كما سبق.
The third chapter: On the divisions of the singular. (Amr) – for if their matter were changed with the particle, the meaning of the proposition would be nullified, as it is said: Zayd in or on, until one says: 'Zayd is in the house' or 'upon Zayd is a dirham.' As for the particle, it is a singular word that cannot independently be made one of the two parts of the proposition, neither subject nor predicate, and in reality it is not used for a meaning in itself, but for a meaning in something else, so it is called an instrument, like our saying: 'min', 'fi', 'ala' and the like. If an objection is raised against what we have indicated concerning the particle, regarding incomplete nouns, pronouns, and existential verbs, from the aspect of the impossibility of their independence with which complete nouns and non-existential verbs are independent, then the proper resolution is to say: As for incomplete nouns like 'alladhi' etc., if one of the two parts of the proposition is prevented, so that it is not said 'alladhi (qa'im)' or 'Zayd alladhi', that is only because the attribution to the vague is presumed, and its attribution – even if it is impossible before specification by the relative clause and the pronoun – is not impossible after it, namely that it is said 'alladhi huwa abyad (qa'im)', so 'alladhi' is the subject in the proposition and 'abyad' is its adjective, and it is not hidden that such a thing is not found in the particle. As for pronouns like 'huwa' and 'hiya' etc., even if it is impossible to make them part of the proposition as it is, it is not said 'huwa qa'im' but rather 'Zayd (qa'im)', that is only because of what we indicated concerning the incomplete; otherwise, the meaning of 'huwa' and 'hiya' in our saying 'Zayd huwa qa'im' or 'Zaynab hiya qa'ima' is none other than Zayd and Zaynab themselves, but not so with particles. As for existential verbs like 'wajada', 'yujadu', 'kana', 'yakunu', and 'ka'in', even if that is also impossible in them, that is only when the intention is to link the subject and predicate as in our saying: 'Zayd exists, or he was, or he is in the world or the city'; otherwise, if the intention is nothing other than informing about his existence and being while what is attributed is from complete nouns, then the statement is complete, and what is like this is impossible in the particle. On this basis, the separation of the noun from the verb and the particle is in the permissibility of composing a declarative proposition from it, like our saying: 'Zayd (qa'im)', and its impossibility in the verb and the particle, for that cannot be composed from them, as it is said: 'qama' (qa'ada), 'min', 'fi', 'ala'. And what separates the verb from the noun and the particle is the necessity of its attribution to the infinitive, and the necessity of external time in its structure. And what separates the particle from the noun and the verb is the impossibility of its being independent as one of the two parts of the proposition, and that its usage is not for a meaning intended in itself, but in something else, as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage defines the three divisions of singular words (المفرد) in logic: the noun (الاسم), the verb (الكلمة), and the particle (الأداة). The author begins by describing the particle, stating that it is a singular word that cannot serve as either subject or predicate in a proposition. Particles are not used for a meaning in themselves, but for a meaning in another word, e.g., 'min', 'fi', 'ala'. The author then addresses an objection concerning apparent exceptions: incomplete nouns (الأسماء النواقص like 'alladhi'), pronouns (المضمرات like 'huwa'), and existential verbs (الكلمات الوجودية like 'kana'). These might seem to behave like particles in that they cannot stand alone as subject or predicate. The author resolves this by explaining that incomplete nouns can become part of a proposition after specification by a relative clause, e.g., 'alladhi huwa abyad qa'im' (the one who is white is standing). Pronouns are not like particles; in 'Zayd huwa qa'im', 'huwa' merely refers back to Zayd. Existential verbs, when used for linking subject and predicate (as copula), do not form a complete proposition by themselves; but when intended to assert existence, they function as predicates. The passage concludes with the distinguishing features: nouns can form a proposition independently; verbs require an infinitive and indicate time; particles cannot be independent parts of a proposition and have meaning only in relation to other words.

Key Terms
الاسم (al-ism): A singular word that can independently serve as subject or predicate in a proposition, e.g., Zayd, qa'im.
الكلمة (al-kalima): A verb; a singular word that requires attribution to an infinitive and includes external time (dhaman khariji) in its structure. [Note: In earlier Arabic grammar, kalima sometimes means any word; here it is specifically the verb in logical terminology.]
الأداة (al-ada): A particle; a singular word that cannot independently form part of a proposition and indicates a meaning in another word.
الأسماء النواقص (al-asma' al-naqisa): Incomplete nouns such as relative pronouns (e.g., alladhi) that cannot be subject or predicate without a relative clause.
المضمرات (al-mudmarat): Pronouns like huwa, hiya; they refer back to a noun and are not independent like particles.
الكلمات الوجودية (al-kalimat al-wujudiyya): Existential verbs like kana, wajada; they can serve as copula linking subject and predicate or assert existence. [Note: In later logic, existential verbs are often analyzed as part of the copula, but here they are differentiated from particles.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the pronoun 'huwa' in 'Zayd huwa qa'im' not considered an 'ada' (particle)?
A) Because 'huwa' can be made subject of a proposition independently.
B) Because 'huwa' has a meaning in itself, namely the person Zayd, unlike particles.
C) Because 'huwa' is an incomplete noun requiring a relative clause.
D) Because 'huwa' functions as a copula linking subject and predicate.

Key Takeaway
The particle (ada) is unique in having no meaning in itself, only in relation to other words, distinguishing it from nouns and verbs.

Univocal and Equivocal Terms
Author defines univocal and equivocal terms with the example of 'aswad'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between univocal (mutawati') and equivocal (mushtarak) terms and identify which category a given term falls into based on its usage.

Core Idea
Univocal terms share one meaning across instances; equivocal terms carry multiple distinct meanings.

In Islamic logic, understanding how a term relates to its meaning is crucial for precise definitions. The author introduces two key categories: univocal terms, which apply in the same respect to multiple referents, and equivocal terms, which apply in different respects. This lesson will help you identify and distinguish these types using concrete examples from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the referents
When you see a term used for multiple things, list those things. For example, the term 'aswad' (black) applied to two black objects.
Focus on the objects, not the term itself.
2
Check the respect
Ask whether the term is used for each referent in exactly the same way or in different ways. For 'aswad', if we consider the colour black, it is the same respect; if we consider the individual essences of the objects, it is different respects.
The same referent can have multiple respects.
3
Classify the term
If the term applies in the same respect to all referents, it is univocal (mutawati'). If it applies in different respects, it is equivocal (mushtarak).
A term can be both univocal and equivocal when considered from different aspects.
4
Handle complex cases
The author notes that a single term can exhibit both univocity and equivocation from different perspectives. In the 'aswad' example, the term is univocal with respect to colour and equivocal with respect to essence.
This complexity does not undermine the classification; it shows the importance of specifying the respect.

إلى نفس النسبة، فإنه إذ ذاك يكون متواطئاً، وقد يتفق أن يجتمع في الاسم الواحد بالنظر إلى مسميئي التواطؤ والاشتراك لكن من جهتين مختلفتين كـ (الأسود) إذا قيل على شيئين متلونين بالسواد، فإن قوله (عليهما) بالنظر إلى ذاتيهما عند جعله لقباً لهما يكون مشتركاً، وقوله (عليهما) بالنظر إلى لونيهما يكون متواطئاً، بل وقوله على الواحد بالنظر إلى هذين المعنيين يكون بالاشتراك أيضاً، وأما إن كان أصليّاً بالنسبة إلى البعض دون البعض فيسمى (مستعاراً) وإطلاق الاسم المستعار على ما استعير له إما بسبب مشابهته لمحل الحقيقة في الصورة والشكل كإطلاق اسم الإنسان على الحيوان الناطق، والمصور على الحائط بسبب ما بينهما من المشابهة في الصورة، وإما أن يكون ذلك بسبب مشابهة في صفة معنوية كإطلاق اسم الأسد على السبع والإنسان نظراً إلى ما اشتركا فيه من صفة الشجاعة، لكن شرط الاستعارة هاهنا أن يكون الاشتراك بين محل الحقيقة والتجوّز في أمر ظاهر مشتهر في محل الحقيقة، حتى لو كان ذلك خفيّاً لم تصح الاستعارة بسببه كإطلاق اسم الأسد على السبع والإنسان بسبب اشتراكهما في صفة البخر، ولو أطلق الاسم باعتباره لما كان في الوضع إلا مشتركاً، وقد يختص هذا القبيل من المستعار بالمشابه وقد تكون الاستعارة بسبب ما بين محل الحقيقة والتجوّز من مجاورة أو ملازمة في الغالب، مثال الأول إطلاق اسم الغائط على الخارج المستقذر من الإنسان لمجاورته للموضع المطمئن من الأرض، واسم القرية على أهلها. ومثال الثاني إطلاق اسم السبب على المسبب كإطلاق اسم الزكاة على الحكم الشرعي، وإطلاق اسم الشيء على ما يؤول إليه كقوله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّكَ مَيِّتٌ ﴾، أو إطلاقه بسبب ما كان عليه كتسمية الرماد خشباً، والإنسان نطفة ونحو ذلك. لكن ما غلب استعماله من الأسماء المستعارة بحيث يكون متبادراً إلى الفهم مهما أطلق وصار محل الحقيقة بالنسبة إليه نسياً منسياً سُمّي (منقولاً) كإطلاق اسم الغائط على الخارج المستقذر من الإنسان.
To the same relation, for then it is univocal. It may happen that in a single name, with respect to two meanings, univocity and equivocation come together but from two different aspects, like (al-aswad) when said of two things colored black. For saying it (with respect to them) with regard to their essences when making it a title for them is equivocal, and saying it (with respect to them) with regard to their colors is univocal. Indeed, saying it of one thing with respect to these two meanings is also equivocal. As for if it is original with respect to some but not others, it is called (metaphorical). The application of a metaphorical name to what it is used metaphorically for is either due to its resemblance to the locus of reality in form and shape, like applying the name 'insan' to the rational animal, and the image on the wall due to the resemblance in form between them; or it is due to a resemblance in a mental attribute, like applying the name 'asad' to the beast and the human with regard to what they share of the quality of courage. But the condition of metaphor here is that the sharing between the locus of reality and the figurative [locus] be in a matter apparent and well-known in the locus of reality, such that if it were hidden, the metaphor would not be valid because of it, like applying the name 'asad' to the beast and the human due to their sharing in the quality of halitosis. If the name were applied in consideration of that, it would only be equivocal in the concept. This category of metaphor may be specific to resemblance, and metaphor may be due to contiguity or habitual concomitance between the locus of reality and the figurative. Example of the first: applying the name 'gha'it' to the filthy excrement from a human due to its contiguity with the low-lying place in the earth, and the name 'qarya' to its inhabitants. Example of the second: applying the name of the cause to the effect, like applying the name 'zakat' to the legal ruling, and applying the name of a thing to what it leads to, like the Almighty's saying: 'Indeed, you are mortal', or applying it due to what it was, like naming ash 'wood', and the human 'sperm', and so on. But what has predominant usage among metaphorical names such that it is immediately understood whenever used and the locus of reality becomes forgotten and obliterated is called (transferred), like applying the name 'gha'it' to the filthy excrement from a human.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes types of single-word relations to their meanings. The author begins by explaining univocal (mutawati') and equivocal (mushtarak) terms. A term is univocal when it applies to multiple referents in exactly the same respect, like 'black' applied to two objects because of their shared blackness. It is equivocal when the same term applies to different referents in different respects, or when applied to one referent from two different aspects. The author then moves to metaphorical terms (musta'ar). A term is metaphorical if it is originally used for one meaning but then applied to another due to some resemblance. Two types of resemblance are given: formal resemblance (e.g., calling a rational animal 'insan' due to likeness in image) and qualitative resemblance (e.g., calling a brave man 'asad' due to courage). The condition for valid metaphor is that the shared quality must be apparent and well-known in the original meaning; otherwise, the term would merely be equivocal. Metaphor can also arise from contiguity (mujawara) or habitual concomitance (mulazama). Examples: 'gha'it' originally means a low-lying place but is used for excrement due to spatial contiguity; 'zakat' originally means purification but is used for the obligatory charity (the legal ruling) as a cause-effect relation. Finally, if a metaphorical term becomes so dominant that the original meaning is forgotten, it is called transferred (manqul), like 'gha'it' for excrement.

Key Terms
متواطئ
مشترك

Checkpoint
In the example of 'aswad' (black) applied to two black objects, when is the term considered equivocal?
A) When considering their color
B) When considering their individual essences
C) When considering both color and essence simultaneously
D) When the term is used for a single object

Key Takeaway
A term is univocal when it applies in the same respect to multiple referents, and equivocal when it applies in different respects; a single term can be both from different angles.

Metaphorical and Transferred Terms
Author defines metaphorical terms, their types, conditions, and introduces transferred terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify metaphorical (musta'ar) terms, state the conditions for their validity, and distinguish them from transferred (manqul) terms.

Core Idea
Metaphorical terms borrow from an original meaning; transferred terms have lost the original meaning entirely.

Beyond univocity and equivocation, the author introduces metaphorical terms (musta'ar), which are applied figuratively based on resemblance or contiguity. If the figurative usage becomes dominant and the original is forgotten, the term is called transferred (manqul). This lesson explains the types of metaphor, the condition for its validity, and how to identify transferred terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary and figurative meanings
For a given term, find the original meaning (locus of reality) and the figurative meaning (what it is used for). For example, 'asad' originally means lion but is used for a brave man.
The original meaning must still be known.
2
Determine the basis of transfer
Check whether the figurative usage is due to resemblance (in form or attribute) or contiguity (spatial, causal, or habitual concomitance). The author gives examples for each.
Resemblance can be in form or in mental attribute.
3
Check the condition for valid metaphor
The shared quality between the original and figurative must be apparent and well-known in the original meaning. If it is hidden, the term becomes equivocal, not metaphorical.
The example of 'asad' based on halitosis fails this condition.
4
Assess if the term is transferred
If the figurative usage becomes so dominant that it is immediately understood and the original meaning is forgotten, the term is called transferred (manqul). The example is 'gha'it' for excrement.
Transferred terms are a subset of metaphorical terms.

True Synonymy vs. False Synonymy
Author defines synonyms and warns against false synonymy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish true synonyms (mutaradif) from terms that share a subject but differ in meaning (mutabayina) and identify common errors.

Core Idea
True synonymy requires identical meaning, not just identical referent.

The author begins by defining synonyms as multiple words for a single entity. He then warns against a common mistake: thinking that words like 'sayf' and 'sarim' are synonyms because they refer to the same sword. Their meanings differ due to additional attributes. Understanding this distinction is crucial for precise communication in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of synonymy
Find where the author states that synonymy requires two words indicating the same thing. Note the examples: 'al-layth' and 'al-asad' for lion.
The definition is clear: unity of denotation, not just referent.
2
Examine the false synonymy example
Look at the example of 'sayf', 'sarim', and 'hindi'. The author explains that each has a different meaning: 'sayf' is the iron tool, 'sarim' adds cutting, 'hindi' adds geographical relation.
These are mutabayina with coinciding subjects.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The author clarifies that unity of subject does not imply synonymy. True synonymy depends on identical meaning, not just pointing to the same object.
4
Apply to new examples
Think of pairs from Islamic sciences: e.g., 'al-fajr' and 'al-subh' (both dawn, but nuances). Decide if they are true synonyms or not.
Use the criterion of identical denotation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان من القسم الثاني وهو ما تكثر فيه الاسم واتحد المسمى فيسمى (مترادفاً) وهو أن يَدُلّ كل واحد من اللفظين على نفس ما دل عليه الآخر كـ (الليث) والأسد) للسبع المفترس، و(الإنسان والبشر) للحيوان الناطق، وقد يقع الغلط بظن الترادف عند اختلاف معاني الأسماء اتحاد موضوعها كـ (السيف) والصارم والهندي ولا يعلم أن الترادف إنما هو بسبب اختلاف الاسم واتحاد مسمّاه، وأن اتحاد موضوع المسمّيات المختلفة مما لا يوجب لها الاتحاد أصلاً. ولا يخفى أن مدلول اسم السيف إنما هو الآلة المتخذة من الحديد على الشكل المصنوع، والصارم اسم لها مع صفة زائدة وهي القطع، والهندي اسم لها نظراً إلى نسبتها إلى الهند. وبالجملة فمهما اختلفت مدلولات الأسماء ومسمياتها متكثرة فتلك (المتباينة) كـ (الماء) و(الهواء) و(التراب) و(النار) ونحوه، وقد تتفق موضوعات مدلولاتها كهذا المثال (الصارم) و(الهندي) فلا تظنها مترادفة وما كان من هذا القبيل من المتباينة فهي إنما تكون للموضوعات مأخوذة من الصفات، إما بطريق الاشتقاق أو النسبة إليها فهي لا تخرج عن أن تكون مشتقة أو منسوبة، فالمشتق كل اسم موضوع لمعنى جعل بتغييره عن شكله، إما بتصريف أو زيادة أو نقصان يدل على ذلك المعنى وعلى موضوع له غير معيّن كقولنا: أسود وأبيض آخذاً من السواد والبياض، حتى إنه لو وضع اسم الصفة لموضوعها من غير تغيير في الشكل لما كان مشتقاً، بل من قبيل ما سُمّي مشتركاً أو مجازاً وذلك كما في إطلاق اسم العدل على الشخص الموجود فيه العدل، بخلاف ما لو سُمّي عادلاً. وأما المنسوبة فكل اسم موضوع لمعنى جعل بشكله مع إلحاق الياء في آخره يدل على موضوعه وعلى ما يرتبط به ذلك كقولنا في المنسوب إلى بغداد بغدادي وإلى دمشق دمشقي ونحو ذلك. والمنتخب في العلوم من الألفاظ إنما هو استعمال المستعارة والمشتركة وما هو من غيرها غريب، إذ هو مما يخل بمقصود الوضع وهو التفاهم، ويبعد عن اقتناص المطلوب عند التخاطب، وقد يقع بسبب ذلك غلط كثير، وأكثر ما يستعمل ذلك في المغالطات.
As for if it is of the second division, which is when the names are many and the named entity is one, it is called (synonymous), meaning that each of the two words indicates the same thing as the other, like (al-layth) and (al-asad) for the predatory beast, and (al-insan) and (al-bashar) for the rational animal. Error may occur by supposing synonymy when the meanings of names differ but their subject is the same, like (al-sayf), al-sarim, and al-hindi. One does not know that synonymy is only due to difference of name and unity of the named entity, and that unity of the subject of different named entities does not require their unity at all. It is not hidden that the denotation of the name 'al-sayf' is only the tool taken from iron in the manufactured shape, and 'al-sarim' is a name for it with an additional attribute, which is cutting, and 'al-hindi' is a name for it considering its relation to India. In summary, whenever the denotations of names differ and their named entities are multiple, they are (different) like (water), (air), (earth), and (fire), and so on. And the subjects of their denotations may coincide, like this example (al-sarim) and (al-hindi); do not think them synonymous. What is of this kind among the different [names] are only taken from attributes for the subjects, either by way of derivation or relation to them; they do not exit from being derived or relative. So the derived term (mushtaqq) is every name placed for a meaning made by changing it from its form, either by conjugation, addition, or deletion, indicating that meaning and an undetermined subject, like our saying: 'aswad' and 'abyad', taken from 'sawad' and 'bayad'. Even if the name of an attribute were placed for its subject without change in form, it would not be derived, but rather of the kind called equivocal or metaphorical, as in applying the name 'al-'adl' to the person in whom justice exists, unlike if he were called 'adil'. As for relative terms (al-mansuba), they are every name placed for a meaning made in its form with the attachment of 'ya' at its end, indicating its subject and what it is connected to, like our saying in the relative to Baghdad 'Baghdadi', and to Damascus 'Dimashqi', and so on. The chosen [usage] in the sciences of words is only the use of metaphorical and equivocal [terms]; what is other than them is strange, for it violates the purpose of conceptualization, which is mutual understanding, and distances from attaining the desired [meaning] in discourse. Much error occurs due to that, and most such usage occurs in sophistries.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage continues the classification of single-word meanings. The author first defines synonyms (mutaradif): two or more words that refer to exactly the same entity in the same respect. Examples: 'al-layth' and 'al-asad' both meaning the lion; 'al-insan' and 'al-bashar' both meaning the rational animal. Then he warns against a common error: thinking that words like 'sayf' (sword), 'sarim' (sharp), and 'hindi' (Indian) are synonyms. They are not, because their denotations differ—'sayf' refers to the iron tool, 'sarim' adds the attribute of cutting, and 'hindi' indicates a geographical relation. Thus, they are actually different (mutabayina) but share a common subject (the sword). The author then explains that such terms are either derived (mushtaqq) or relative (mansub). A derived term is one formed from another word with a change in form (e.g., 'aswad' from 'sawad'—blackness). If no change, it is either equivocal or metaphorical, like calling a just person 'al-'adl' (justice) instead of 'adil' (just). A relative term is formed by adding a 'ya' suffix to indicate relation, like 'Baghdadi' from Baghdad. Finally, the author notes that in scientific discourse, one should avoid metaphorical and equivocal terms as they hinder clear understanding and are primarily used in fallacious arguments.

متباينة

Checkpoint
Why are 'al-sayf' and 'al-sarim' not considered synonyms?
A) Because they differ in gender
B) Because they have different meanings despite referring to the same object
C) Because one is derived and the other is relative
D) Because 'al-sarim' is a metaphor

Key Takeaway
True synonymy requires identical meaning (mudlul), not merely shared subject (mawdu').

Derived Terms and Relative Terms
Author defines derived and relative terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify derived (mushtaqq) and relative (mansub) terms and explain why some attributive names are not derived.

Core Idea
A derived term requires a change in form; otherwise it is equivocal or metaphorical.

After discussing synonyms, the author explains two categories of terms that describe attributes: derived terms (mushtaqq) and relative terms (mansub). He gives conditions for each and warns against using non-standard terms in scholarly discourse. This lesson sharpens your ability to analyze word forms in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of derivation
A derived term (mushtaqq) is formed from a root by changing its shape (e.g., adding letters, conjugation). It indicates an attribute and an unspecified subject. Example: 'aswad' from 'sawad'.
The form change is essential; without it, the term is not derived.
2
Distinguish derived from non-derived attributive names
If a name for an attribute is used for its subject without changing form, it is not derived—it is either equivocal (mushtarak) or metaphorical (majaz). Example: calling a just person 'al-'adl' (justice) instead of 'adil' (just).
The author emphasizes that 'al-'adl' used for a just person is not a derived term.
3
Define relative terms
A relative term (mansub) is formed by adding a 'ya' suffix to indicate relation to a place, tribe, etc. Example: 'Baghdadi' from Baghdad.
The 'ya' is essential; without it, the term is not relative.
4
Apply the criteria to new terms
Given a list of terms like 'katib' (writer), 'Qurashi' (from Quraysh), 'sadiq' (truthful), decide if each is derived, relative, or neither.
Use the conditions: form change for derived, 'ya' suffix for relative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان من القسم الثاني وهو ما تكثر فيه الاسم واتحد المسمى فيسمى (مترادفاً) وهو أن يَدُلّ كل واحد من اللفظين على نفس ما دل عليه الآخر كـ (الليث) والأسد) للسبع المفترس، و(الإنسان والبشر) للحيوان الناطق، وقد يقع الغلط بظن الترادف عند اختلاف معاني الأسماء اتحاد موضوعها كـ (السيف) والصارم والهندي ولا يعلم أن الترادف إنما هو بسبب اختلاف الاسم واتحاد مسمّاه، وأن اتحاد موضوع المسمّيات المختلفة مما لا يوجب لها الاتحاد أصلاً. ولا يخفى أن مدلول اسم السيف إنما هو الآلة المتخذة من الحديد على الشكل المصنوع، والصارم اسم لها مع صفة زائدة وهي القطع، والهندي اسم لها نظراً إلى نسبتها إلى الهند. وبالجملة فمهما اختلفت مدلولات الأسماء ومسمياتها متكثرة فتلك (المتباينة) كـ (الماء) و(الهواء) و(التراب) و(النار) ونحوه، وقد تتفق موضوعات مدلولاتها كهذا المثال (الصارم) و(الهندي) فلا تظنها مترادفة وما كان من هذا القبيل من المتباينة فهي إنما تكون للموضوعات مأخوذة من الصفات، إما بطريق الاشتقاق أو النسبة إليها فهي لا تخرج عن أن تكون مشتقة أو منسوبة، فالمشتق كل اسم موضوع لمعنى جعل بتغييره عن شكله، إما بتصريف أو زيادة أو نقصان يدل على ذلك المعنى وعلى موضوع له غير معيّن كقولنا: أسود وأبيض آخذاً من السواد والبياض، حتى إنه لو وضع اسم الصفة لموضوعها من غير تغيير في الشكل لما كان مشتقاً، بل من قبيل ما سُمّي مشتركاً أو مجازاً وذلك كما في إطلاق اسم العدل على الشخص الموجود فيه العدل، بخلاف ما لو سُمّي عادلاً. وأما المنسوبة فكل اسم موضوع لمعنى جعل بشكله مع إلحاق الياء في آخره يدل على موضوعه وعلى ما يرتبط به ذلك كقولنا في المنسوب إلى بغداد بغدادي وإلى دمشق دمشقي ونحو ذلك. والمنتخب في العلوم من الألفاظ إنما هو استعمال المستعارة والمشتركة وما هو من غيرها غريب، إذ هو مما يخل بمقصود الوضع وهو التفاهم، ويبعد عن اقتناص المطلوب عند التخاطب، وقد يقع بسبب ذلك غلط كثير، وأكثر ما يستعمل ذلك في المغالطات.
As for if it is of the second division, which is when the names are many and the named entity is one, it is called (synonymous), meaning that each of the two words indicates the same thing as the other, like (al-layth) and (al-asad) for the predatory beast, and (al-insan) and (al-bashar) for the rational animal. Error may occur by supposing synonymy when the meanings of names differ but their subject is the same, like (al-sayf), al-sarim, and al-hindi. One does not know that synonymy is only due to difference of name and unity of the named entity, and that unity of the subject of different named entities does not require their unity at all. It is not hidden that the denotation of the name 'al-sayf' is only the tool taken from iron in the manufactured shape, and 'al-sarim' is a name for it with an additional attribute, which is cutting, and 'al-hindi' is a name for it considering its relation to India. In summary, whenever the denotations of names differ and their named entities are multiple, they are (different) like (water), (air), (earth), and (fire), and so on. And the subjects of their denotations may coincide, like this example (al-sarim) and (al-hindi); do not think them synonymous. What is of this kind among the different [names] are only taken from attributes for the subjects, either by way of derivation or relation to them; they do not exit from being derived or relative. So the derived term (mushtaqq) is every name placed for a meaning made by changing it from its form, either by conjugation, addition, or deletion, indicating that meaning and an undetermined subject, like our saying: 'aswad' and 'abyad', taken from 'sawad' and 'bayad'. Even if the name of an attribute were placed for its subject without change in form, it would not be derived, but rather of the kind called equivocal or metaphorical, as in applying the name 'al-'adl' to the person in whom justice exists, unlike if he were called 'adil'. As for relative terms (al-mansuba), they are every name placed for a meaning made in its form with the attachment of 'ya' at its end, indicating its subject and what it is connected to, like our saying in the relative to Baghdad 'Baghdadi', and to Damascus 'Dimashqi', and so on. The chosen [usage] in the sciences of words is only the use of metaphorical and equivocal [terms]; what is other than them is strange, for it violates the purpose of conceptualization, which is mutual understanding, and distances from attaining the desired [meaning] in discourse. Much error occurs due to that, and most such usage occurs in sophistries.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage continues the classification of single-word meanings. The author first defines synonyms (mutaradif): two or more words that refer to exactly the same entity in the same respect. Examples: 'al-layth' and 'al-asad' both meaning the lion; 'al-insan' and 'al-bashar' both meaning the rational animal. Then he warns against a common error: thinking that words like 'sayf' (sword), 'sarim' (sharp), and 'hindi' (Indian) are synonyms. They are not, because their denotations differ—'sayf' refers to the iron tool, 'sarim' adds the attribute of cutting, and 'hindi' indicates a geographical relation. Thus, they are actually different (mutabayina) but share a common subject (the sword). The author then explains that such terms are either derived (mushtaqq) or relative (mansub). A derived term is one formed from another word with a change in form (e.g., 'aswad' from 'sawad'—blackness). If no change, it is either equivocal or metaphorical, like calling a just person 'al-'adl' (justice) instead of 'adil' (just). A relative term is formed by adding a 'ya' suffix to indicate relation, like 'Baghdadi' from Baghdad. Finally, the author notes that in scientific discourse, one should avoid metaphorical and equivocal terms as they hinder clear understanding and are primarily used in fallacious arguments.

منسوب

Classifying Mental Necessary Concomitants
Author begins to classify necessary mental concomitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify mental necessary concomitants as mutual or one-sided, and as immediate or mediated.

Core Idea
Not every concomitant is mutual: some are one-sided, and some require a middle term.

In logic, necessary concomitants are properties that necessarily accompany an essence. This passage reveals that these concomitants come in different types: some are mutual, some one-sided; some are immediate, others require a chain of intermediate properties. Understanding this classification helps avoid infinite regress and sharpens our analysis of essential and accidental relations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main division
The author divides mental necessary concomitants based on the direction of necessity: mutual (both sides) or one-sided.
2
Mutual concomitants: essential vs accidental
Mutual concomitants can be essential (based on the essences themselves, like the relation between relatives) or accidental (one is cause of the other, or both are effects of a common cause, like laughing and writing).
3
One-sided concomitants: immediate vs mediated
One-sided concomitants are either immediate (no middle term, like individuality for three) or mediated (the essence implies a property, which implies another property).
4
Understand the infinite regress warning
If every concomitant required a middle term, there would be infinite regress, which is impossible. Therefore, some concomitants must be immediate.
5
Apply to a new example
Take the concept 'human' and its concomitant 'rational'. Is it mutual or one-sided? Does it have a middle term? Apply the criteria.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما اللازم الذهني فاللزوم بينه وبين ملزومه إما أن يكون من الجانبين أو أحدهما، فإن كان من الجانبين فإما أن يكون لذاتيهما كاللزوم بين المضافين وإما لا لذاتيهما وذلك بأن يكون أحدهما علة للمعلول الآخر، أو هما معلولان لعلة خارجية وهما متساويان في العموم والخصوص كملازمة الضاحك للكاتب وإن كان من أحد الجانبين فمنه ما لا واسطة له بين اللازم وبين بين اللازم وبين الماهية الملزومة ولا بد من الاعتراف به، وإلا فلو كان كل لازم له وسط لتسلسل الأمر وهو محال، وذلك كملازمة الفردية للثلاثة ونحوه، ومنه ما وسط وهو أن تكون الماهية مقتضية لصفة وتلك الصفة مقتضية لصفة أخرى، ومنه ما تكون الماهية مقتضية تلك الصفة مقتضية لصفة أخرى، فالأول كملازمة مساواة الزاويتين لصفة وهي مع
As for the mental concomitant, the necessity between it and its thing to which it is necessary is either from both sides or one side. If from both sides, then either (the necessity) is essential to them, like the necessity between relatives, or not essential to them, and that is by one of them being a cause of the other effect, or both are effects of an external cause and they are equal in generality and particularity, like the concomitance of 'laughing' and 'writer'. And if it is from one side, then among it is what has no middle term between the concomitant and between [repetition?] the essence to which it is necessary, and it must be acknowledged; otherwise, if every concomitant had a middle term, infinite regress would occur, which is impossible. That is like the concomitance of 'individuality' for three and the like. And among it is what has a middle term, which is that the essence necessitates a property, and that property necessitates another property, and among it is what the essence necessitates that property which necessitates another property. The first is like the concomitance of the equality of the two angles to a property, and it is with...
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage begins a classification of necessary concomitants (lazim). The author focuses on mental concomitants, i.e., logical or ideological necessities. First, the necessity (luzum) between the concomitant and that to which it is necessary (malzum) can be mutual (both sides) or one-sided. If mutual, it can be essential (based on the essences themselves, like the relation between relatives) or not essential (where one is a cause of the other, or both are effects of a common cause, as with the concomitance of the properties 'laughing' and 'writer' in humans). If one-sided, then either the concomitant has no middle term (immediate necessity, like 'individuality' for three) or it has a middle term (where the essence implies a property, which in turn implies another). The author warns that accepting a middle term for every concomitant leads to infinite regress, so there must be some immediate necessities. The passage is cut off mid-example about the equality of angles. This classification is important for understanding the types of accidental properties that are inseparable but not essential.

Key Terms
لازم ذهني (lazim dhihni): A mental necessary concomitant; a notion that necessarily accompanies another in the mind. [Note: This term is used in logic to distinguish from ontological concomitants; later texts may use 'lazim aqli'.]
اللزوم (luzum): The relation of necessity between the concomitant and the malzum; can be essential or accidental. [Note: Stable; also used in usul al-fiqh for implications.]
ملزوم (malzum): The thing to which the concomitant is necessary; the subject or essence. [Note: Standard term in logic.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because immediate concomitants are more certain than mediated ones.
D) Because the author says so without justification.

Key Takeaway
The classification of necessary concomitants into mutual/one-sided and immediate/mediated is essential for understanding the structure of logical necessity and avoiding infinite regress.

Resolving the Objection about the Essential Universal
Author presents and resolves an objection against the essential universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and resolve the objection that the essential universal cannot be universal.

Core Idea
The essential universal does not exist in particulars as a universal; it is a correspondence of realities.

A common objection claims that if the universal is essential to the particulars, it would have to exist in them as a universal, which seems impossible. The author clarifies that the reality of 'essential' here is not about inherence but about correspondence. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the logical concept of the essential universal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first part of the passage (يوجب أن يكون موجوداً فيها...وحتى محال). What does the objector assume about the essential universal?
The objector assumes that being essential requires existence in the particular as a separate universal entity.
2
Locate the author's response
Find the phrase 'قلنا: ليس المراد...' and note how the author redefines what it means for a universal to be essential.
The author says it means the reality that became universal corresponds to the specific reality constitutive of the particular.
3
Understand the example
The author gives the example of 'animal' (الحيوان). How does 'animal' as a universal relate to the specific animal in Zayd and Amr?
The universal 'animal' corresponds to the specific animal nature that is part of the definition of 'human' in Zayd and Amr.
4
Confirm the resolution
Why does this interpretation resolve the impossibility? Explain in your own words.
It shows that the universal's being essential does not require it to exist as a separate entity; it is a conceptual correspondence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يوجب أن يكون موجوداً فيها، ضرورة كونه مقوماً لها، ولا يلزم من ذلك ألا يكون كلياً؛ لاستحالة انحصار ما يصلح للشركة في ما لا يصلح للشركة، أو ألا تكون الجزئيات المشتركة فيها جزئيات وكل ذلك محال، وهذا المحال إنما لزم من كون الكلي ذاتياً للجزئيات فيكون محالاً. قلنا: ليس المراد من قولنا إن الكلي ذاتي للجزئيات أنـه مـن حيـث هو كلي موجود فيها ومقوم لها، بل معناه أن حقيقة ما عرض له أن صار كلياً كـ (الحيوان) مثلاً مطابقاً لما وجد من الحيوان الخاص المقوم للإنسان الموجود في زيد وعمرو فاندفع الإشكال. وأما العرضي فهو ما لا يتوقف تصور موضوعه في معناه على تصوره أولاً، ومن فهم سر ما أشرنا إليه من التقييد في جانب رسم الذاتي لم يخف عليه نقيضه هاهنا، بل وأمكنه أيضاً تبيّن فساد قول من قال في رسمه هو ما لا يمتنع سلبه عن تصور الشيء لظنه أن العرضي لا يكون ملازماً وليس كذلك
It would necessitate that it exists in them, due to the necessity of it being constitutive of them, and it does not follow from that that it is not universal; because it is impossible for that which is suitable for sharing to be confined to that which is not suitable for sharing, or that the particulars that share in it are not particulars. All of that is impossible, and this impossibility only follows from the universal being essential to the particulars, so it is impossible. We say: What is meant by our statement that the universal is essential to the particulars is not that it, qua universal, exists in them and is constitutive of them, but rather its meaning is that the reality that acquired the property of becoming universal, like (animal) for example, corresponds to what is found of the specific animal that is constitutive of the human existing in Zayd and Amr, so the difficulty is repelled. As for the accidental, it is that whose subject's conception in its meaning does not depend on its conception first, and whoever understands the secret of what we pointed to of the restriction in the definition of the essential will not be unaware of its opposite here, rather he can also perceive the corruption of the statement of one who said in its definition that it is that whose negation from the conception of a thing is not impossible, because he thought that the accidental is not necessary concomitant, and it is not like that.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange followed by a definition. The author begins by presenting an objection: if the universal is essential to the particulars, it would have to exist in them and be constitutive, leading to impossibilities (e.g., that the universal would not be universal). The author then responds by clarifying that the universal's being essential does not mean it exists in particulars as a universal; rather, it means that the reality which becomes universal (e.g., the animal nature) corresponds to the specific animal that constitutes the human in Zayd and Amr. This correspondence does not imply the universal's existence as a separate entity. The term 'essential' here refers to the correspondence of the conceptual reality, not inherence. The objection is thus resolved. Next, the author defines the accidental (al-'aradi): it is that whose conception does not depend on prior conception of its subject. The author then critiques a mistaken definition that claims the accidental is that whose negation is not impossible, arguing that such a definition conflates the accidental with the non-necessary. The key is that the accidental is not necessarily separable in all respects; it can be a concomitant (lazim) in some ways. This passage guards against misunderstanding the essential as existent in particulars and the accidental as always separable.

الكلي
الجزئي

Key Takeaway
The essential universal does not inhere in particulars as a separate entity; it is a conceptual correspondence that constitutes the particular's definition.

Defining the Accidental and Correcting a Mistake
Author defines the accidental and critiques a mistaken definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to correctly define the accidental (al-'aradi) and identify a common mistaken definition.

Core Idea
The accidental is that whose conception does not depend on prior conception of its subject.

After clarifying the essential, the author defines the accidental and critiques a mistaken view that equates the accidental with what is not necessary. Understanding the precise definition is crucial for distinguishing essential from accidental attributes in definitions and arguments.

Guided Path
1
Extract the correct definition
Find the phrase 'وأما العرضي فهو ما لا يتوقف تصور موضوعه في معناه على تصوره أولاً'. What does it mean for the accidental's conception not to depend on the subject's conception?
It means you can conceive the accidental without first conceiving the subject it belongs to.
2
Contrast with the mistaken definition
The author mentions a mistaken definition: 'هو ما لا يمتنع سلبه عن تصور الشيء' (that whose negation from the conception of a thing is not impossible). Why is this insufficient?
Because it assumes the accidental is always separable, but some accidents can be necessary concomitants (lazim).
3
Understand the author's critique
The author says the mistaken definition is based on the belief that the accidental is not a necessary concomitant. Why is that belief wrong?
Some accidents, like 'risibility' for humans, are necessary concomitants even though they are not part of the essence.
4
Formulate the correct understanding
Write the correct definition in your own words and give an example of an accidental that is a necessary concomitant.
Example: 'Ability to laugh' for humans is accidental (not part of essence) but necessarily accompanies human nature.

يوجب أن يكون موجوداً فيها، ضرورة كونه مقوماً لها، ولا يلزم من ذلك ألا يكون كلياً؛ لاستحالة انحصار ما يصلح للشركة في ما لا يصلح للشركة، أو ألا تكون الجزئيات المشتركة فيها جزئيات وكل ذلك محال، وهذا المحال إنما لزم من كون الكلي ذاتياً للجزئيات فيكون محالاً. قلنا: ليس المراد من قولنا إن الكلي ذاتي للجزئيات أنـه مـن حيـث هو كلي موجود فيها ومقوم لها، بل معناه أن حقيقة ما عرض له أن صار كلياً كـ (الحيوان) مثلاً مطابقاً لما وجد من الحيوان الخاص المقوم للإنسان الموجود في زيد وعمرو فاندفع الإشكال. وأما العرضي فهو ما لا يتوقف تصور موضوعه في معناه على تصوره أولاً، ومن فهم سر ما أشرنا إليه من التقييد في جانب رسم الذاتي لم يخف عليه نقيضه هاهنا، بل وأمكنه أيضاً تبيّن فساد قول من قال في رسمه هو ما لا يمتنع سلبه عن تصور الشيء لظنه أن العرضي لا يكون ملازماً وليس كذلك
It would necessitate that it exists in them, due to the necessity of it being constitutive of them, and it does not follow from that that it is not universal; because it is impossible for that which is suitable for sharing to be confined to that which is not suitable for sharing, or that the particulars that share in it are not particulars. All of that is impossible, and this impossibility only follows from the universal being essential to the particulars, so it is impossible. We say: What is meant by our statement that the universal is essential to the particulars is not that it, qua universal, exists in them and is constitutive of them, but rather its meaning is that the reality that acquired the property of becoming universal, like (animal) for example, corresponds to what is found of the specific animal that is constitutive of the human existing in Zayd and Amr, so the difficulty is repelled. As for the accidental, it is that whose subject's conception in its meaning does not depend on its conception first, and whoever understands the secret of what we pointed to of the restriction in the definition of the essential will not be unaware of its opposite here, rather he can also perceive the corruption of the statement of one who said in its definition that it is that whose negation from the conception of a thing is not impossible, because he thought that the accidental is not necessary concomitant, and it is not like that.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange followed by a definition. The author begins by presenting an objection: if the universal is essential to the particulars, it would have to exist in them and be constitutive, leading to impossibilities (e.g., that the universal would not be universal). The author then responds by clarifying that the universal's being essential does not mean it exists in particulars as a universal; rather, it means that the reality which becomes universal (e.g., the animal nature) corresponds to the specific animal that constitutes the human in Zayd and Amr. This correspondence does not imply the universal's existence as a separate entity. The term 'essential' here refers to the correspondence of the conceptual reality, not inherence. The objection is thus resolved. Next, the author defines the accidental (al-'aradi): it is that whose conception does not depend on prior conception of its subject. The author then critiques a mistaken definition that claims the accidental is that whose negation is not impossible, arguing that such a definition conflates the accidental with the non-necessary. The key is that the accidental is not necessarily separable in all respects; it can be a concomitant (lazim) in some ways. This passage guards against misunderstanding the essential as existent in particulars and the accidental as always separable.

الذاتي
الملازمة

The accidental is defined by the independence of its conception from its subject, not by its separability.

Classification of Necessary Concomitant: Mental and Existential
Classification of necessary concomitant and defense of simple having concomitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mental and existential necessary concomitance and identify their subtypes (bilateral, unilateral, permanent, non-permanent).

Core Idea
The necessary concomitant is not a single type — it splits into mental and existential, each with further divisions.

The author expands on the concept of 'necessary concomitant' (al-lazim), a key tool in definition and proof. He classifies it into mental and existential, then further divides mental into bilateral and unilateral, and existential into permanent and non-permanent. He also refutes the claim that a simple entity cannot have a concomitant, using the example of the number one. This classification helps avoid conflating logical and ontological categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary division of lazim
Note that the author first divides lazim into mental (al-lazim al-dhihni) and existential (al-lazim al-wujudi). The mental is based on conceptual necessity; the existential is based on external existence.
The mental existential distinction is fundamental.
2
Analyze the subtypes of mental lazim
Mental lazim is either bilateral (from both sides) or unilateral (from one side). Bilateral further splits into essential (e.g., correlatives) and non-essential (e.g., laughing and writer). Unilateral splits into those without an intermediary (e.g., oddness for three) and those with an intermediary (e.g., equality of two angles for a triangle).
The examples are crucial for understanding.
3
Examine the subtypes of existential lazim
Existential lazim is either permanent (e.g., blackness for the crow) or non-permanent. Non-permanent is slow (e.g., youth) or fast (e.g., standing).
These are based on temporal duration in external existence.
4
Refute the claim that a simple cannot have a concomitant
The author argues that a simple (basit) can have multiple concomitants through external relations, using the example of the number one having many fractions. He also notes that the concomitant may be caused by an external factor, not the essence.
This refutes a common misconception.
5
Clarify the term 'accidental' (al-'aradi)
The author distinguishes the logical accidental (which can be substantial, like 'laughing' as a property of a substance) from the ontological accident (which requires a subject).
This avoids category confusion.

(السواد) للقـار والغـراب والبياض للثلج والفضة، وأما غير الدائم فإما بطيء الزوال أو سريع، فالأول كـ (الشباب)، والثاني كـ (القيام والقعود ونحوه. وما حققناه في اللازم الواحد فلازم في ما زاد على الواحد، ولا فرق في ذلك أن يكون الملزوم بسيطاً أو مركباً، ولا التفات إلى من زعم أن البسيط لا لازم له، وإلا كان قابلاً وفاعلاً وإن كان له لازم ولا يلزمه لازمان لامتناع صدور الكثرة عن البسيط، أما الأول فالجواز أن يكون لازم البسيط معلولاً لأمر خارج، وإن كان البسيط قابلاً له، كيف وإن القبول والتأثير غير خارج عن النسب والإضافات والبسيط الواحد لا يمتنع عليه النسب المتعددة والإضافات المختلفة كـ (الواحد) الذي هو مبدأ العدد فإنه نصف الإثنين وثلث الثلاثة وربع الأربعة وهلم جراً، وأما الثاني فالجواز أن يكونا معلولين لخارج أو أحدهما للذات والآخر لخارج، ولا تتوهمنّ ما قد يتوهمه بعض المقصرين من أن المراد بالعرضي ما هو قسيم الجوهر كما يأتي تحقيقه، فإن ذلك من حيث هو ما هو لا يكون إلا في موضوع على تحققه، والعرضي إما سمي عرضياً؛ لكون ما اتصف به مستغنياً عنه في معناه، وهو أعم من المفتقر إلى الموضوع، فإنه قد يكون عن الموضوع مستغنياً كالضاحك والكاتب، فإن الضاحك والكاتب هو الشيء الذي قام به الضحك والكتابة، وهو غير مفتقر إلى الموضوع؛ لكونه جوهرياً، وأما ما يفتقر من العرضيات إلى الموضوع فكالضحك والكتابة وغير ذلك من العرضيات التي لا يصح حملها إلا بطريق الاشتقاق، أو بإدخال حرف (ذو) وكون ما سمي من الشيء الموصوف بالضحك عارضاً للإنسان، وإن اتفق إن كان ذلك الشيء في نفس الأمر هو حقيقة الإنسان التي قيل أنه عارض لها، فكونه عارضاً إنما كان من جهة تعقله شيئاً مطلقاً قابلاً
As for the mental necessary concomitant, the concomitance between it and its subject is either from both sides or one side. If from both sides, then either it is due to their essences, like the concomitance between correlatives, or not due to their essences, such as one being a cause of the other effect, or both being effects of an external cause while they are equal in generality and particularity, like the concomitance of 'laughing' and 'writer'. If from one side, then some have no intermediary between the concomitant and the subject essence, and it must be acknowledged, otherwise if every concomitant had a middle, it would lead to infinite regress, which is impossible; such as the concomitance of 'oddness' with 'three' and the like. Some have an intermediary, which is that the essence necessitates a property and that property necessitates another property; some have the essence necessitating a property, and that property necessitating another. The first is like the concomitance of the equality of two angles (a property) with the right angles for the three angles of a triangle, for it is concomitant to the triangle by means of the three angles being concomitant to the triangle. The second is like the concomitance of the acceptance of increase and decrease for the essence of quantity by virtue of its being divisible. In summary, such a division is not impossible, even if the examples multiply. As for the existential concomitant without the mental (wahm), it is either permanent or non-permanent. The permanent is like blackness for the crow and raven, and whiteness for snow and silver. The non-permanent is either slow to disappear or fast; the first is like youth, the second is like standing and sitting and the like. What we have verified for a single concomitant applies to more than one, and there is no difference in that between the subject being simple or composite. No attention should be paid to one who claims that the simple has no concomitant, otherwise it would be both receptive and agent. And if it has a concomitant, it does not entail two concomitants because multiple effects cannot proceed from a simple. As for the first, it is possible that the concomitant of the simple is an effect of an external cause, even if the simple is receptive of it. Indeed, receptivity and influence are not outside relations and additions; a single simple is not prevented from having multiple relations and different additions, like the 'one' which is the principle of number, for it is half of two, third of three, quarter of four, and so on. As for the second, it is possible that both are effects of an external cause, or one for the essence and the other for an external cause. Do not imagine what some short-sighted imagine, that what is meant by 'accidental' is what is contrasted with substance, as will be investigated. For that, insofar as it is what it is, only exists in a subject upon its realization. The accidental is called accidental because the thing described by it is independent of it in its meaning; it is more general than what depends on a subject, for it may be independent of a subject like 'laughing' and 'writer', for the laugher and writer is the thing in which laughter and writing inhere, and it is not dependent on a subject because it is substantial. As for what depends on a subject among the accidentals, it is like laughter and writing and other accidentals which cannot be predicated except by derivation or by entering the particle 'dhu' (possessor of), and calling the thing described by laughter an accident of the human, even if that thing in reality is the reality of the human which is said to be that to which it is accidental. Its being accidental is only from the perspective of conceiving it as an absolute thing that is receptive [of it].
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of the necessary concomitant (al-lazim). The author begins by dividing the mental concomitant (al-lazim al-dhihni) according to the direction of concomitance: bilateral or unilateral. Bilateral concomitance is further divided into essential (e.g., correlatives like father and son) and non-essential (where one is cause of the other, or both are effects of a common cause, as with 'laughing' and 'writer'). Unilateral concomitance is divided into that without an intermediary (e.g., oddness for three) and that with an intermediary (e.g., equality of two angles for a triangle, mediated by the three angles). The author then discusses the existential concomitant (al-lazim al-wujudi) without the mental, dividing it into permanent (e.g., blackness for the crow) and non-permanent (slow like youth, fast like standing). Next, the author addresses the common notion that a simple (basit) entity cannot have a necessary concomitant, refuting it by showing that a simple can have multiple concomitants through external relations (e.g., the number one having multiple fractions). The author then clarifies that the term 'accidental' (al-'aradi) is not to be confused with the ontological accident (al-'arad) opposed to substance; rather, the logical accidental is defined by independence of meaning, and it can be either substantial (like 'laughing' as a property of a substance) or properly accidental (like laughter itself, which requires a subject). This classification guards against conflating logical and ontological categories.

Refuting the Simple Distinction Between Essential and Accidental
Author critiques the mistaken criterion for distinguishing essential and accidental attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain why the criterion of removability fails to distinguish essential from accidental attributes, using the author's examples.

Core Idea
Some accidents cannot be removed even in imagination, disproving the absolute separation criterion.

In Islamic logic, distinguishing essential (dhātī) from accidental (ʿaraḍī) attributes is crucial. Most logicians claim accidents can be conceptually removed from the essence. But this passage shows that some accidents are necessarily concomitant. Understanding this nuance prevents errors in definition and reasoning across the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mistaken claim
Locate where the author states that someone who uses 'these two criteria' absolutely has erred. What is the claim being critiqued? It is that accidents are always separable from the essence either in reality or in imagination.
The criteria likely refer to removability and dependence on an external cause.
2
Examine the first counterexample: human's preparedness for knowledge
The author says: 'whoever makes man to be man has made him prepared to receive knowledge.' This preparedness is an accident, yet it cannot be imagined without the essence. Why? Because the definition of man includes rational animal, and rationality entails the capacity for knowledge. Thus, stripping man of this capacity would destroy the essence.
3
Examine the second counterexample: individuation of three
The author says: 'whoever makes it three has made it individuated.' Three is a number; its being individuated (a single three) is inseparable. You cannot conceive of three without it being a single unit. Thus, individuation is an accident that necessarily accompanies the essence.
This example illustrates that even abstract accidents can be inseparable.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that the absolute criterion for distinguishing essential from accidental by removability is false. Some accidents are necessarily concomitant. Therefore, one must refine the distinction: essential is that which enters the definition; accidental is that which does not, even if inseparable.
This sets up the author's subsequent discussion of divisions of essential and accidental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السادس: في قسمة الكلي إلى الذاتي والعرضي وإذا ثبت ذلك فالواجب أن نعلم أن من رام التفرقة بين الذاتي والعرضي بهذين الفرقين مطلقاً فقد أخطأ، إذ من العوارض ما لا يمكن سلبه عن الشيء وجوداً ولا وهماً، بل ومنها ما لا يمكن القول بلحوقه بالذات لعلة وُجدت لولاها لما كان لاحقاً بالذات، ككون الإنسان مستعداً لقبول العلم فإن من جعل الإنسان إنساناً فقد جعله مستعداً لقبول العلم، وكما في الفردية بالنسبة إلى الثلاثة، فإن من جعلها ثلاثة فقد جعلها فرداً حتى لو أردنا تجرُّدَ الإنسان عن استعداد العلم، أو الثلاثة عن الفردية لقد كان ذلك ممتنعاً. وإذ أتينا على ما أردناه من بيان معنى الذاتي والعرضي، فنرجع إلى ما هو المقصود من بيان أقسام الذاتي، وبيان أقسام العرضي، ولتكن البداية بالذاتي لما تقدم
Chapter Six: On the Division of the Universal into Essential and Accidental. And when that is established, it is necessary to know that whoever attempts to distinguish between the essential and the accidental by these two criteria absolutely has erred. For among accidents are those whose removal from the thing is impossible in existence or in imagination; rather, among them are those for which it cannot be said that they attach to the essence due to a cause which, if it had not existed, they would not attach to the essence. Like the preparedness of man to receive knowledge: for whoever makes man to be man has made him prepared to receive knowledge. And like individuation (fardiyya) with respect to three: for whoever makes it three has made it individuated (fardan). So much so that if we wanted to strip man of the preparedness for knowledge, or three of individuation, that would be impossible. And since we have come to what we intended in explaining the meaning of essential and accidental, we return to what is the purpose: explaining the divisions of the essential and the divisions of the accidental. Let the beginning be with the essential, due to what has preceded.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a critique of a specific method for distinguishing essential (ذاتي) from accidental (عرضي) attributes. The author begins by stating that whoever uses 'these two criteria' absolutely is mistaken. The criteria likely refer to a previous discussion. The author then provides two examples to show that some accidental attributes cannot be removed from the essence even in imagination, nor do they attach due to an external cause separable from the essence. The first example: human's preparedness for knowledge (استعداد العلم). If you make something human, you have made it prepared for knowledge; you cannot conceive of a human without that preparedness. Yet preparedness for knowledge is not part of the definition of humanity? According to the author, it is an accident that nevertheless necessarily accompanies the essence. The second example: individuation (فردية) with respect to the number three. If you make something three, it is necessarily individuated; you cannot have three without it being a single three (fard). Thus these accidents are inseparable, disproving the criterion that accidents can be stripped from the essence. The author then transitions to his intended discussion: explaining the divisions of essential and accidental, starting with essential.

Key Takeaway

The 'What' Question: Why Genus Alone Does Not Suffice
Author defines 'what' question and begins division of essential

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a complete and incomplete answer to the logical 'what' question, and identify the role of genus and difference in defining quiddity.

Core Idea
Answering 'what is it?' requires both genus and specific difference, not just the common essential.

In Islamic logic, asking 'what is it?' (mā huwa) seeks the complete essence (quiddity) of a thing. Some mistakenly think that naming the shared genus alone suffices, e.g., calling man, horse, and ox simply 'animal'. But the author of Daqāʾiq al-Ḥaqāʾiq argues that the common essential does not indicate the specific differentia, and thus fails to disclose the true reality. This lesson unpacks his critique and introduces the logical division into genus and species.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logical question
Recognize that 'what is it?' (mā huwa) is the tool for seeking quiddity. In logic, the response must fully disclose the essence without neglecting any constituent.
The passage stresses the need for 'disclosure of its essence completely'.
2
Extract the author's criticism
The author rejects the view that the answer can be only the common essential (e.g., 'animal' for man and horse). He argues that the common essential does not indicate the specific difference, so it cannot capture the complete reality.
He says: 'the more general does not indicate the more specific'.
3
Distinguish common vs. specific essential
The common essential (genus) is shared across species; the specific essential (difference) distinguishes each species. A complete answer includes both – e.g., 'rational animal' for man.
The author uses the term 'al-dhātī al-khāṣṣ' for the specific essential.
4
Apply the distinction to examples
For a single individual (Zayd), the answer is 'rational animal'. For a group of species (man, horse, ox), the answer is 'animal' – note that this is incomplete for any single species within the group.
The passage clarifies that the answer for a group is not sufficient for an individual because of the individual's specific essence.
5
Recognize the definition of genus
The author begins the formal division: if an essential is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' on many things differing in essence, it is called genus (jins). If on many differing in accident, it is species (nawʿ). The passage ends mid-sentence with this definition.
This sets up the classical logical hierarchy.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السابع في بيان انقسام الذاتي إلى الجنس والنوع والفصل وتحقيق كل واحد منها وقبل الخوض في ذلك لا بُدّ من تحقيق معنى الدال على الماهية والمقول في ما وليس والمقول في جواب ما غير ما تدل على الماهية والكشف عن جوهرها على سبيل الكمال، من غير إهمال لبعض مقوماتها، كما لو سأل عن الإنسان بما هو؟ فقال: حيوان ناطق، وعن إنسان وفرس وثور؟ فقال: حيوان، لا كما ظن قوم أنه الذاتي المشترك لا غير، فإن الطالب لها إنما طلبه الكشف عن الحقيقة، وليست الحقيقة على ما هي بما لها من الذاتي المشترك فقط، حتى يصح الاقتصار في الجواب عليه؛ إذ ليس هو كل مفهوم الحقيقة ولا لدلالة له على الذاتي الخاص منه، إذ لا دلالة للأعم على الأخص كيف وأن فصول الأجناس ذاتيات مشتركة، ولا يقال في جواب ما، ولا في الدلالة على الماهية كما ستعرفه، وإن أريد بالذاتي المشترك ما يرجع إلى ما له دلالة على كمال الماهية وأعرض عما هو المفهوم عند المشهور فهو المقصود، ولا فرق في هذا بين أن يكون السؤال عما هو واحد بالذات كـ (زيد) أو بالنوع كـ (زيد وعمرو) أو بالجنس كـ (الإنسان والفرس) إلا أن ما يقع في الجواب عن الجماعة الأولى يكون بعينه هو الجواب عن واحدها، فإن ما وقع به اختصاص كل واحد ليس إلا بعارض لا بذاتي، ومما يقع في الجواب عن الجماعة الثانية فلا يكون كافياً عن الواحد منها لتخصصه بـ (زيد) في ذاته، وسيأتي تحقيق هذا بزيادة شرح وإيضاح في ما بعد إن شاء الله تعالى. وعند هذا فنقول: الذاتي المقول إما أن يكون مقولاً على المشتركات فيه في جواب ما أو ليس فإن كان مقولاً في جواب ما فإما أن يكون مقولاً على كثيرين مختلفين بالذات أو العرض فإن كان الأول فهو المسمى (جنساً) وإن كان الثاني فهو
Chapter Seven: On Explaining the Division of the Essential into Genus, Species, and Difference, and Verification of Each of Them. Before delving into that, we must verify the meaning of that which indicates the quiddity, and the predicated in answer to 'what is it' and not, and the predicated in answer to 'what is it' other than what indicates the quiddity, and the disclosure of its essence completely, without neglecting some of its constituents, as if one asks about man 'what is he?' and he says: 'rational animal', and about man, horse, and ox he says: 'animal', not as some people think that it is only the common essential. For the seeker of them only seeks the disclosure of the reality, and the reality is not as it is with only the common essential it has, so that one may suffice with the answer on it; since it is not the whole concept of reality, nor does it indicate the specific essential of it, since the more general does not indicate the more specific, especially since the differentiae of genera are common essentials, and they are not said in answer to 'what', nor in indicating the quiddity, as you will know. And if by 'common essential' is meant that which refers to indicating the perfection of the quiddity and disregards what is understood by the well-known, then that is the intended meaning. And there is no difference in this between the question being about what is one in essence like (Zayd), or one in species like (Zayd and Amr), or one in genus like (man and horse), except that what occurs in the answer to the first group is exactly the answer to the single one of them, for what occurs by which each one is specified is only accidental, not essential. And what occurs in the answer to the second group is not sufficient for the answer to the single one of them, because of its specification by (Zayd) in its essence. And there will come a verification of this with more explanation and clarification later, God willing. And at this we say: The essential that is predicated is either predicated on the commonalities in answer to 'what' or not. If it is predicated in answer to 'what', then either it is predicated on many that differ in essence or in accident. If it is the first, then it is called 'genus'. And if it is the second, then it is... [species]
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage from Chapter Seven deals with the division of the essential into genus, species, and difference. The author begins by stressing the need to understand the term that indicates the quiddity (ماهية) and the predicate in answer to 'what is it?'. He clarifies that the answer to 'what is it?' must fully disclose the essence, not merely give a common essential (الذاتي المشترك). He criticizes a group that believes the answer is only the common essential. Using examples: for an individual human, the answer is 'rational animal' (both genus and difference); for a group of different species, the answer is 'animal' (genus alone). He points out that the common essential does not capture the specific essence (الذاتي الخاص). The differentiae (فصول) of genera are themselves common essentials but are not said in answer to 'what is it?'. Then he reinterprets 'common essential' as that which indicates the perfection of the quiddity, if we disregard the common usage. He discusses how questions about one individual, one species, or one genus are answered differently. Finally, he begins the formal division: the essential predicated is either predicated in answer to 'what is it?' or not. If it is predicated in answer to 'what is it?', it is either predicated on many differing in essence (genus) or differing in accident (species). The passage ends with the start of the definition of species. This passage is crucial for understanding the logical categories of genus and species, and for avoiding the error of thinking that only the common genus suffices to answer 'what is it?'.

Key Terms
الذاتي المشترك
الذاتي الخاص
جواب ما

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the author reject the view that the answer to 'what is it?' is only the common essential (الذاتي المشترك)?
A) Because the common essential is not a real essential.
B) Because the common essential does not indicate the specific difference of the quiddity.
C) Because the common essential is a universal.
D) Because the common essential is always false.

Key Takeaway
A complete answer to 'what is it?' in logic requires both the common essential (genus) and the specific essential (difference); the genus alone is insufficient.
