Definition and Distinction of Species
Author defines species and clarifies its logical properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the logical species from other predicables and from the Greek concept of species.

Core Idea
Species is relative to genus; it is always universal and answers 'what is it?'.

Having understood genus, we now turn to its correlative: species. This passage defines species and carefully distinguishes it from other predicables like differentia, property, and accidental universal. It also compares the logical species with the Greek notion based on form. Understanding these distinctions is essential for accurate classification in logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining characteristic
Find where the author states that species is said in answer to 'what is it?' (جواب ما). This is the key property shared with genus but not with other predicables.
This distinguishes species from differentia, property, and accidental universal.
2
Distinguish from other predicables
Notice that species is universal and said in answer to 'what is it?', whereas differentia, property, and accidental universal are not – they answer 'which one?' or 'what is it like?'.
3
Compare logical and Greek species
The author contrasts the logical species (مضايف للجنس) – relative to genus – with the Greek species based on form (صورة). Logical species is always universal and correlative to genus.
Greek species does not require a correlative genus.
4
Understand the infinite regress argument
Read the argument: if understanding a predication of something under a species implied another predication above it, there would be infinite genera. This shows why species must be relative.
This supports the relational definition of species.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جواب ما، فلا تكون أنواعاً بالنسبة إليها، وأما تمييزه عن الفصل والخاصة والعرض العام، والأصناف، فلكونه مقولاً في جواب ما، وما تمييزه عن النوع باستعمال المنطقيين فلنسبة إلى ما فوقه، وعن الأشخاص فلكونه كلياً، ومـا لـه الإنسان بالنسبة إلى الحيوان والحيوان بالنسبة إلى الجسم ذي النفس، وذو النفس بالنسبة إلى الجسم والجسم بالنسبة إلى الجوهر، وهو مخالف للنوع بالمعنى اليوناني من جهة أن نوعيته إنما هي بالنسبة إلى ما فوقه، وذلك إنما نوعيته بالنظر إلى صورته كانت مضافة أم لا، وقد يخالفه من جهة أنه لا يكون إلا كلياً ومقولاً في جواب ما وما مخالفته للنوع الآخر من الوضع المنطقي فلأنه مضايف للجنس والأول غير مضايف، إذ لا يلزم من فهم كون الشيء مقول على ما تحته فهم معنى آخر مقولاً عليه وإلا لما كانت الأجناس العالية إلا بأجناس إلى غير النهاية وهو محال.
وأيضاً فإنه ينقسم إلى نوع عال وسافل ومتوسط، فالعالي هو ما تحته أنواع ولا نوع فوقه كـ (الجسم) فإنه ينقسم إلى الأنواع وما فوقه إنما هو الجوهر وما فوق الجوهر ليس بجنس كما يأتي، فلا يكون البتة نوعاً، وأما النوع السافل فهو ما فوقه نوع وما تحته ليس بأنواع كـ (الإنسان والفرس) فإن ما فوقهما إنما هو الحيوان وهو نوع بالنسبة إلى الجسم ذي النفس وما تحت الإنسان والفرس ليس غير الأصناف والأشخاص لا غير كما سبق، وأما المتوسط فهو ما بين نوعين أي فوقه نوع وتحته نوع كـ (الجسم ذي النفس) فإنه نوع بالنسبة إلى الجسم، والجسم بالنسبة إلى الجوهر، وما تحت الجسم ذي النفس من الحيوان فهو نوع بالنسبة إلى الجوهر، وأما النوع بالاعتبار الأول فإنه لا يقع في نفس الأمر إلا سافلاً، ولا يكون البتة جنساً، بخلاف النوع بالاعتبار الثاني، إلا أن ما كان من هذا النوع سافلاً فإنه يسمى (نوعاً) بالنظر إلى الاعتبارين، وأما ما سمي ذلك فلا، إلا أن ينظر إلى ما ذكرناه من الاشتراط، وهو أن نقطع النظر عن الفصول المتنوعة فلا يتصور أن يكون تحته ما هو نوع فلا يتصور أن يكون نوعاً عالياً ولا متوسطاً، فعلى هذا الجنس منه ما جنس ولا يكون البتة نوعاً كـ (الجوهر) ويسمى (جنس الأجناس) ومنه ما يكون نوعاً هو جنساً نوعاً بالنسبة إلى ما فوقه وجنساً بالنسبة إلى ما تحته، وهو كما في السافل والمتوسط. وأما النوع فمنه ما يكون نوعاً ولا يكون البتة جنساً كالنوع السافل ويسمى (نوع الأنواع) ومنه ما يكون نوعاً وجنساً كما في المتوسط، والعالي فلا يكون نوعاً مطلقاً ولا جنساً مطلقاً، بل هو جنس ونوع كما مضى، ويفارق هذا النوع للنوع بالاعتبار الأول أيضاً في أنه لا يكون إلا مركباً من جنس وفصل ولا كذلك الأول.
Answer to 'what is it?', so they are not species in relation to them. As for distinguishing it from differentia, property, and accidental universal, it is because it is said in answer to 'what is it?'. And what distinguishes it from species in the usage of logicians is because of its relation to what is above it. And from individuals, it is because it is universal. And what it has is man in relation to animal, and animal in relation to ensouled body, and ensouled in relation to body, and body in relation to substance. And it is different from species in the Greek sense in that its species-ness is only in relation to what is above it, while that one's species-ness is with regard to its form, whether it is relative or not. And it may differ from it in that it is only universal and said in answer to 'what is it?'. And its difference from the other species of the logical sense is because it is correlative to genus, while the first is not correlative, for from understanding a thing as predicated on what is under it, it does not necessitate understanding another meaning predicated on it; otherwise, the highest genera would have genera to infinity, which is impossible. And also, it is divided into high, low, and intermediate species. The high species is that under which there are species and no species above it, like body, for it is divided into species, and what is above it is only substance, and what is above substance is not a genus as will come, so it is never a species. As for the low species, it is that above which there is a species and under which there are no species, like man and horse, for above them is only animal, and animal is a species in relation to ensouled body, and under man and horse there are only individuals and persons as previously. As for the intermediate, it is that between two species, i.e., above it a species and under it a species, like ensouled body, for it is a species in relation to body, and body in relation to substance, and what is under ensouled body, namely animal, is a species in relation to substance. As for species in the first sense, it occurs in reality only as low, and is never a genus, unlike species in the second sense, except that what is low from this type is called a species with regard to both senses, while what is not so called is not, unless we consider the condition we mentioned, which is to cut off consideration of the diverse differentiae, so it is not conceivable that under it is what is a species; thus it cannot be conceived as high or intermediate. Accordingly, of genus, there is what is genus and is never a species, like substance, called 'genus of genera', and of it what is a species that is both a genus and a species in relation to what is above it and a genus in relation to what is under it, as in the low and intermediate. As for species, of it there is what is species and is never a genus, like the low species, called 'species of species', and of it what is species and genus as in the intermediate, and the high is neither species absolutely nor genus absolutely, but it is genus and species as passed. And this species differs from the species in the first sense also in that it is only composed of genus and differentia, while the first is not.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'species' (النوع) as used in logic and distinguishes it from other predicables. The author begins by stating that species is said in answer to 'what is it?' (جواب ما), which distinguishes it from differentia, property, and accidental universal, which are not said in answer to that question. He then distinguishes the logical species from the Greek concept of species: logical species is relative to what is above it (i.e., its genus), whereas Greek species is based on the form of the thing. Logical species is always universal and correlative to genus, whereas Greek species is not necessarily correlative. The author argues that if understanding a thing as predicated on what is under it implied understanding another meaning predicated on it, there would be an infinite regress, which is impossible.

Next, the author divides species into three types: high (عالي), low (سافل), and intermediate (متوسط). The high species has species under it but no species above it, e.g., 'body' (الجسم) – there are species like mineral, plant, animal under it, but above it is only substance, which is not a species. The low species has a species above it but no species under it, e.g., 'man' (الإنسان) and 'horse' (الفرس) – above them is 'animal' (الحيوان), but under them are only individuals. The intermediate species is between two species, having a species above and a species below, e.g., 'ensouled body' (الجسم ذي النفس) – it is a species relative to body, and body is a species relative to substance, while animal is a species under it. The author then clarifies that species in the first sense (منطقي) is always low and never a genus, whereas species in the second sense (يوناني) can be low or intermediate. He further explains that if we set aside consideration of differentiae, what is low in the second sense is called a species in both senses, but not otherwise. Finally, he distinguishes the two senses: species in the second sense is composed of genus and differentia, while species in the first sense is not necessarily composed.

Throughout, examples are drawn from the category of substance: 'body', 'ensouled body', 'animal', 'man'. The passage is an expansion of the standard definition of species and its hierarchical division, which is crucial for understanding the Porphyrian tree and categorical classification.

Key Terms
النوع
الجنس
الفصل
الخاصة
العرض العام

Key Takeaway
Logical species is defined by its relation to genus and its answer to 'what is it?', distinguishing it from other predicables and from the Greek species concept.

Division of Species into High, Low, and Intermediate
Author divides species into high, low, and intermediate with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given species is high, low, or intermediate based on its position in a hierarchical chain.

Core Idea
Species can be high (above all species), low (below all species), or intermediate (between two species).

Now that we understand what species is, the author shows that not all species are equal. They occupy different levels in the tree of categories. Using the substance hierarchy, he illustrates three types: high, low, and intermediate. This division is crucial for understanding the Porphyrian tree and categorical logic.

Guided Path
1
Examine the substance hierarchy
Trace the chain: جوهر (substance) -> جسم (body) -> جسم ذي نفس (ensouled body) -> حيوان (animal) -> إنسان (man).
This chain is the author's running example.
2
Define high species (نوع عال)
Identify what is a high species: it has species under it but no species above it. In the chain, 'body' is high because above it is only substance (not a species).
High species is a genus to those below but not a species to anything above.
3
Define low species (نوع سافل)
Identify low species: it has a species above it but no species below it. 'Man' and 'horse' are low because under them are only individuals.
Low species is the most specific; it is never a genus.
4
Define intermediate species (نوع متوسط)
Find the intermediate: it has both a species above and a species below. 'Ensouled body' is intermediate because it is between body (above) and animal (below).
Intermediate species is both a species and a genus.
5
Note the two senses of species
The author distinguishes species in the first sense (logical, relative) from the second sense (Greek, compositional). The division into high/low/intermediate applies only to the second sense.
Species in the first sense is always low and never a genus.

جواب ما، فلا تكون أنواعاً بالنسبة إليها، وأما تمييزه عن الفصل والخاصة والعرض العام، والأصناف، فلكونه مقولاً في جواب ما، وما تمييزه عن النوع باستعمال المنطقيين فلنسبة إلى ما فوقه، وعن الأشخاص فلكونه كلياً، ومـا لـه الإنسان بالنسبة إلى الحيوان والحيوان بالنسبة إلى الجسم ذي النفس، وذو النفس بالنسبة إلى الجسم والجسم بالنسبة إلى الجوهر، وهو مخالف للنوع بالمعنى اليوناني من جهة أن نوعيته إنما هي بالنسبة إلى ما فوقه، وذلك إنما نوعيته بالنظر إلى صورته كانت مضافة أم لا، وقد يخالفه من جهة أنه لا يكون إلا كلياً ومقولاً في جواب ما وما مخالفته للنوع الآخر من الوضع المنطقي فلأنه مضايف للجنس والأول غير مضايف، إذ لا يلزم من فهم كون الشيء مقول على ما تحته فهم معنى آخر مقولاً عليه وإلا لما كانت الأجناس العالية إلا بأجناس إلى غير النهاية وهو محال.

Answer to 'what is it?', so they are not species in relation to them. As for distinguishing it from differentia, property, and accidental universal, it is because it is said in answer to 'what is it?'. And what distinguishes it from species in the usage of logicians is because of its relation to what is above it. And from individuals, it is because it is universal. And what it has is man in relation to animal, and animal in relation to ensouled body, and ensouled in relation to body, and body in relation to substance. And it is different from species in the Greek sense in that its species-ness is only in relation to what is above it, while that one's species-ness is with regard to its form, whether it is relative or not. And it may differ from it in that it is only universal and said in answer to 'what is it?'. And its difference from the other species of the logical sense is because it is correlative to genus, while the first is not correlative, for from understanding a thing as predicated on what is under it, it does not necessitate understanding another meaning predicated on it; otherwise, the highest genera would have genera to infinity, which is impossible. And also, it is divided into high, low, and intermediate species. The high species is that under which there are species and no species above it, like body, for it is divided into species, and what is above it is only substance, and what is above substance is not a genus as will come, so it is never a species. As for the low species, it is that above which there is a species and under which there are no species, like man and horse, for above them is only animal, and animal is a species in relation to ensouled body, and under man and horse there are only individuals and persons as previously. As for the intermediate, it is that between two species, i.e., above it a species and under it a species, like ensouled body, for it is a species in relation to body, and body in relation to substance, and what is under ensouled body, namely animal, is a species in relation to substance. As for species in the first sense, it occurs in reality only as low, and is never a genus, unlike species in the second sense, except that what is low from this type is called a species with regard to both senses, while what is not so called is not, unless we consider the condition we mentioned, which is to cut off consideration of the diverse differentiae, so it is not conceivable that under it is what is a species; thus it cannot be conceived as high or intermediate. Accordingly, of genus, there is what is genus and is never a species, like substance, called 'genus of genera', and of it what is a species that is both a genus and a species in relation to what is above it and a genus in relation to what is under it, as in the low and intermediate. As for species, of it there is what is species and is never a genus, like the low species, called 'species of species', and of it what is species and genus as in the intermediate, and the high is neither species absolutely nor genus absolutely, but it is genus and species as passed. And this species differs from the species in the first sense also in that it is only composed of genus and differentia, while the first is not.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'species' (النوع) as used in logic and distinguishes it from other predicables. The author begins by stating that species is said in answer to 'what is it?' (جواب ما), which distinguishes it from differentia, property, and accidental universal, which are not said in answer to that question. He then distinguishes the logical species from the Greek concept of species: logical species is relative to what is above it (i.e., its genus), whereas Greek species is based on the form of the thing. Logical species is always universal and correlative to genus, whereas Greek species is not necessarily correlative. The author argues that if understanding a thing as predicated on what is under it implied understanding another meaning predicated on it, there would be an infinite regress, which is impossible.

Next, the author divides species into three types: high (عالي), low (سافل), and intermediate (متوسط). The high species has species under it but no species above it, e.g., 'body' (الجسم) – there are species like mineral, plant, animal under it, but above it is only substance, which is not a species. The low species has a species above it but no species under it, e.g., 'man' (الإنسان) and 'horse' (الفرس) – above them is 'animal' (الحيوان), but under them are only individuals. The intermediate species is between two species, having a species above and a species below, e.g., 'ensouled body' (الجسم ذي النفس) – it is a species relative to body, and body is a species relative to substance, while animal is a species under it. The author then clarifies that species in the first sense (منطقي) is always low and never a genus, whereas species in the second sense (يوناني) can be low or intermediate. He further explains that if we set aside consideration of differentiae, what is low in the second sense is called a species in both senses, but not otherwise. Finally, he distinguishes the two senses: species in the second sense is composed of genus and differentia, while species in the first sense is not necessarily composed.

Throughout, examples are drawn from the category of substance: 'body', 'ensouled body', 'animal', 'man'. The passage is an expansion of the standard definition of species and its hierarchical division, which is crucial for understanding the Porphyrian tree and categorical classification.

النوع السافل
النوع المتوسط
الجسم
الإنسان
الجسم ذي النفس

Species are not all at the same level; they can be high, low, or intermediate, depending on their position in the categorical hierarchy.

Types of Differentia: al-Fasl al-'Amm, al-Khass, and Khass al-Khass
Author classifies differentia into three types based on essentiality and permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given attribute into one of the three types of differentia (common, special, most special) based on whether it is essential, permanent accidental, or temporary accidental.

Core Idea
The three types of differentia depend on whether the distinguishing attribute is essential, permanent accidental, or temporary accidental.

In this passage, the author explains that in the Greek logical tradition, the term 'differentia' (fasl) was used broadly for any attribute that distinguishes one thing from another, not just essential ones. He then classifies these distinguishing attributes into three categories based on two criteria: whether the attribute is essential or accidental, and if accidental, whether it is permanent or temporary. Understanding this classification helps us appreciate the precise usage of 'fasl' in later Islamic logic and its application in definition theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the attribute
Consider the distinguishing attribute you want to classify. Is it an essential part of the thing's nature, or is it an accident that can change?
Essential attributes form the quiddity; accidents are additional.
2
Check essentiality
If the attribute is essential, it belongs to the most special differentia (khass al-khass). If accidental, proceed to the next step.
3
Check permanence for accidents
If the accidental attribute is permanent and inseparable from the subject, it is special differentia (al-fasl al-khass). If it is temporary and separable, it is common differentia (al-fasl al-'amm).
Permanent accidents may be inborn (e.g., flat nose) or acquired later but become fixed (e.g., scars).
4
Apply the classification
Use the two-step decision tree: first essential vs accidental; for accidentals: permanent vs temporary. Then label the attribute accordingly.
The author also provides alternative schemes starting with permanence, but the result is the same.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفصل ففي لغة اليونان ما يوجب تمييز شيء عن غيره، كان ذلك المميز ذاتياً لما ميزه أو عرضياً، لكن ما كان منه من العرض مفارقاً، كانوا يخصونه بالفصل العام إذ هو مما يصح أن يتميز به كل واحد من الشخصين عن صاحبه في وقتٍ، بل الشخص الواحد بالنسبة إلى أوقات متعددة وسواء كان ذلك سريع الزوال كـ (القيام) والقعود أو بطيء الزوال كـ (الشباب). فإنه لا يخفى أن من تميز عن غيره بكونه قائماً عند كون ذلك الغير قاعداً أنه قد يتميز الآخر عنه إذا قعد هو وقام ذلك الغير، بل وقد يختلف حال الشخص الواحد به بالنسبة إلى أوقات كان في بعضها قائماً وفي بعضها قاعداً، ومثله في الشباب أيضاً، ولذلك سمي (فصلاً عاماً)، وما كان منه دائماً غير مفارق فكانوا يخصونه بـ (الفصل الخاص) وسواء كان ذلك ثابتاً في أصل الخلقة كـ (الشهولة) أو (الزرقة) في العين، أو لاحقاً عارضاً كـ (آثار الجراحات المندملة) ونحوها، وما كان منه ذاتياً فإنهم كانوا يعبرون عنه بـ (خاص الخاص). وإن شئت أن نلخص عبارة في حصر هذه الأقسام الثلاثة، قلت إما أن يكون ذاتياً أو عرضياً، فإن كان ذاتياً فهو خاص الخاص وإن كان عرضياً فهو إما دائم أو غير دائم، فإن كان الأول فهو الخاص وإن كان الثاني فهو العام. وإن شئت قلت: إما دائم أو غير دائم، فإن كان دائماً فهو جوهري وإما عرضي، فإن كان جوهرياً فخاص الخاص، وإن كان عرضياً فإما دائم أو غير دائم، فإن كان دائماً فخاص، وإن كان غير دائم فعام وللعبارات في ذلك متسع.
As for the differentia (al-fasl), in the Greek language it is that which necessitates distinguishing a thing from another, whether that distinguisher is essential for what it distinguishes or accidental. But what is found among it as separable accident, they would allocate it to the common differentia, because it is that by which each one of the two persons can be distinguished from his companion at a time, indeed the same person in relation to multiple times, whether that is quickly passing like standing and sitting, or slowly passing like youth. For it is not hidden that whoever is distinguished from another by being standing when that other is sitting, then the other may be distinguished from him when he sits and that other stands, indeed the state of the same person may differ with it in relation to times; in some he is standing, in some he is sitting. And similarly for youth. So they called it 'common differentia'. And what among it is permanent and inseparable, they would allocate it to 'special differentia', whether that is fixed in the original creation like flat nose or blueness of the eye, or a subsequent accidental like the effects of healed wounds and the like. And what among it is essential, they would express it as 'most special'. And if you wish to summarize a statement in encompassing these three divisions, I say: it is either essential or accidental. If essential, then it is most special; if accidental, then it is either permanent or non-permanent. If the first, then it is special; if the second, then it is common. And if you wish, I say: it is either permanent or non-permanent. If permanent, then it is either substantial or accidental. If substantial, then it is most special; if accidental, then it is either permanent or non-permanent. If permanent, then it is special; if non-permanent, then it is common. And there is scope for expressions in that.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage classifies the differentia (الفصل) into three types according to the Greek tradition, based on whether the attribute is essential or accidental and whether it is permanent or temporary. The author begins by defining differentia in the Greek language as anything that distinguishes one thing from another, whether that distinguishing attribute is essential (ذاتي) or accidental (عرضي). He then divides accidental attributes into separable (مفارق) and inseparable (غير مفارق).

The first type is the 'common differentia' (الفصل العام), which is a separable accident, meaning it can leave the subject. Examples include 'standing' (القيام) and 'sitting' (القعود) which are quick to change, and 'youth' (الشباب) which changes slowly but still leaves. Such an attribute can distinguish one person from another at a given moment, but the distinction is temporary.

The second type is the 'special differentia' (الفصل الخاص), which is an inseparable accident – it always stays with the subject but is not essential to its quiddity. Examples given are 'flat nose' (الشهولة) and 'blueness of the eye' (الزرقة), which are present from original creation, as well as 'effects of healed wounds' (آثار الجراحات المندملة) which are acquired later but become permanent.

The third type is the 'most special differentia' (خاص الخاص), which is the essential differentia – it constitutes the essence of the thing. The author then provides two summarizing classifications. The first: if the attribute is essential, it is most special; if accidental, it is either permanent (then special) or non-permanent (then common). The second classification: first divide into permanent and non-permanent; if permanent, then divide into substantial (then most special) and accidental (then special); if non-permanent, then it is common.

Throughout, the author uses examples from everyday life and physical characteristics. This classification is important because in logic, the term 'differentia' usually refers specifically to the essential differentia (خاص الخاص), but this passage acknowledges broader usages.

Key Terms
الفصل
الفصل العام
الفصل الخاص
خاص الخاص

Checkpoint

A) Blueness of the eye (الزرقة في العين)
B) Rationality (الناطق)
C) Standing (القيام)
D) Flat nose (الشهولة)

Key Takeaway
The differentia can be classified into three types—common (temporary accidental), special (permanent accidental), and most special (essential)—based on essentiality and permanence.

?

Simple and Composite Universals
Author distinguishes simple and composite universals and refutes a misunderstanding about essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish simple from composite universals and correctly predicate the composite form using derivation or 'possessor of'.

Core Idea
Simple universals are abstract nouns; composites are derived words used in definitions.

The five universals each have a simple (abstract) and a composite (derived) form. The simple form cannot be directly predicated; we must use derivation or 'possessor of'. This distinction is crucial for correct logical usage in definitions and arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the simple form
Find the abstract noun (e.g., 'animality', 'humanity', 'rationality'). These end in -iyya in Arabic.
2
Test direct predication
Try to predicate the simple form directly, e.g., 'Man is animality'. Observe that it is grammatically and logically incorrect.
Direct predication of an abstract noun is invalid.
3
Convert to composite form
Use derivation (e.g., 'animal' from 'animality') or the phrase 'possessor of' (e.g., 'dhū ḥayawāniyya'). Now predication is correct: 'Man is an animal' or 'Man is possessor of animality'.
The composite form is the derived word itself.
4
Apply in logical contexts
Only composite universals are used in definitions (ḥadd) and syllogisms. The simple forms are accidental to the subject.
This is why logicians focus on composites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

They also share in that each one of them has both a simple and a composite form. The simple is what is predicated only by way of derivation or by inserting the word 'possessor of', such as 'animality', 'humanity', 'rationality', 'laughter', and 'blackness'. For it cannot be said 'Man is animality' nor 'Zayd is humanity'; rather, that is only correct if one says 'Man is an animal' or 'possessor of animality'. And similarly for the rest of the five universals. As for the composite, it is what is indicated by the derived word from the simple, like 'animal' and 'man' and the like. They also all share in that the logical among them is only the composite, not the simple, and that the simple in all of them is accidental to what it describes, not substantial. And there is a common mistake among those with short sight in making this statement, because they think that animality and rationality are essential for man and substantial for him, because the understanding of man is not conceived except when animality and the power of rationality are first understood. They do not know that the essential for man is only the thing described by animality and rationality, and that the priority of rationality and animality in understanding is only because they are concomitant in understanding what is prior in understanding, since they are an introduction to it, not because they enter into substantiality and meaning. This is similar to the priority in understanding of the properties of genera that are concomitant to them over the understanding of their species. As for examining the agreement and difference in each rank that results from the combination of these five, it would be lengthy for this book to explain. But we will restrict ourselves to explaining the aspect of agreement and difference between every two of them. From that, one can become aware of the aspect in every rank resulting from them. Let the beginning be with explaining the aspect of agreement and difference between genus and species, then
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the five universals, now focusing on their division into simple and composite forms. The author states that each universal has a simple and a composite version. The simple form is an abstract noun like 'animality' (al-ḥayawāniyya) or 'humanity' (al-insāniyya), which cannot be directly predicated of a subject; instead, we must use a derived word or the phrase 'possessor of' (dhū). For example, we cannot say 'Man is animality' but must say 'Man is an animal' (using the composite 'animal') or 'Man is a possessor of animality'. The composite form is the derived word itself, like 'animal' or 'man'. The author then explains that the logical universals used in definitions are the composite forms, not the simple ones. The simple forms are accidental (ʿarḍī) to what they describe, not substantial (jawhar). This leads to a common misunderstanding: those with limited insight think that simple forms like 'animality' and 'rationality' are essential and substantial for man because they are prior in understanding—we cannot conceive 'man' without first conceiving 'animality' and 'rationality'. The author refutes this by clarifying that what is truly essential is the subject (e.g., the human) that possesses these attributes. The priority of these attributes in understanding is not because they are part of the essence, but because they are necessary concomitants (lāzima) that serve as an introduction to understanding the essence, similar to how the properties of a genus are understood before the species. Finally, the author notes that a full treatment of the agreement and difference among the five universals in various combinations would be lengthy, so he will focus only on pairwise comparisons, beginning with genus and species.

Simple universals are abstract nouns that cannot be directly predicated; they must be converted to composite forms via derivation or 'possessor of'.

The Common Mistake: Mistaking Concomitants for Essence
Author refutes the misunderstanding about simple universals being essential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the error of treating simple universals as essential and substantial based on their priority in understanding, and correctly distinguish essence from concomitant.

Core Idea
Priority in understanding does not make an attribute essential; the essence is the subject, not the attribute.

Some mistakenly think that simple universals like 'animality' and 'rationality' are essential for man because they are understood first. The author refutes this by showing that these are mere concomitants that introduce the essence, not parts of it. This distinction is vital for correct metaphysical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mistaken claim
Find where the author states that short-sighted people think animality and rationality are essential (dhātī) and substantial (jawharī) for man because they are prior in understanding.
The mistaken view arises from conflating epistemic priority with ontological constitution.
2
Understand the refutation
The author clarifies that the essential is the thing described (e.g., man), not the attributes. The attributes are merely descriptive of the essence.
3
Recognize the role of concomitants
Animality and rationality are necessary concomitants (lāzima) that are understood first as an introduction to the essence, but they are not part of the essence.
They are like the properties of a genus that are understood before the species.
4
Apply the analogy
Just as the properties of a genus are prior in understanding but not essential, so too are simple universals like animality.
This refutes the mistake by analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

They also share in that each one of them has both a simple and a composite form. The simple is what is predicated only by way of derivation or by inserting the word 'possessor of', such as 'animality', 'humanity', 'rationality', 'laughter', and 'blackness'. For it cannot be said 'Man is animality' nor 'Zayd is humanity'; rather, that is only correct if one says 'Man is an animal' or 'possessor of animality'. And similarly for the rest of the five universals. As for the composite, it is what is indicated by the derived word from the simple, like 'animal' and 'man' and the like. They also all share in that the logical among them is only the composite, not the simple, and that the simple in all of them is accidental to what it describes, not substantial. And there is a common mistake among those with short sight in making this statement, because they think that animality and rationality are essential for man and substantial for him, because the understanding of man is not conceived except when animality and the power of rationality are first understood. They do not know that the essential for man is only the thing described by animality and rationality, and that the priority of rationality and animality in understanding is only because they are concomitant in understanding what is prior in understanding, since they are an introduction to it, not because they enter into substantiality and meaning. This is similar to the priority in understanding of the properties of genera that are concomitant to them over the understanding of their species. As for examining the agreement and difference in each rank that results from the combination of these five, it would be lengthy for this book to explain. But we will restrict ourselves to explaining the aspect of agreement and difference between every two of them. From that, one can become aware of the aspect in every rank resulting from them. Let the beginning be with explaining the aspect of agreement and difference between genus and species, then
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the five universals, now focusing on their division into simple and composite forms. The author states that each universal has a simple and a composite version. The simple form is an abstract noun like 'animality' (al-ḥayawāniyya) or 'humanity' (al-insāniyya), which cannot be directly predicated of a subject; instead, we must use a derived word or the phrase 'possessor of' (dhū). For example, we cannot say 'Man is animality' but must say 'Man is an animal' (using the composite 'animal') or 'Man is a possessor of animality'. The composite form is the derived word itself, like 'animal' or 'man'. The author then explains that the logical universals used in definitions are the composite forms, not the simple ones. The simple forms are accidental (ʿarḍī) to what they describe, not substantial (jawhar). This leads to a common misunderstanding: those with limited insight think that simple forms like 'animality' and 'rationality' are essential and substantial for man because they are prior in understanding—we cannot conceive 'man' without first conceiving 'animality' and 'rationality'. The author refutes this by clarifying that what is truly essential is the subject (e.g., the human) that possesses these attributes. The priority of these attributes in understanding is not because they are part of the essence, but because they are necessary concomitants (lāzima) that serve as an introduction to understanding the essence, similar to how the properties of a genus are understood before the species. Finally, the author notes that a full treatment of the agreement and difference among the five universals in various combinations would be lengthy, so he will focus only on pairwise comparisons, beginning with genus and species.

عرضي (ʿaraḍī)
جوهر (jawhar)
لازمة (lāzima)

Restrictive vs Predicative Compounds
Author defines restrictive and predicative compounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between restrictive and predicative compounds and classify any given expression as beneficial for attaining concept (tasawwur) or assent (tasdiq).

Core Idea
Only two compound types yield knowledge: definitions (restrictive) and propositions (predicative).

Having studied simple terms, we now examine how they combine. The author explains that only two types of compound are useful for acquiring concept and assent: the restrictive compound, which forms definitions, and the predicative compound, which forms propositions. All other compounds—commands, questions, etc.—do not directly give knowledge. This lesson will teach you to identify each type.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two goals of logic
The author states that the purpose of studying compounds is to obtain tasawwur (concept) and tasdiq (assent). Keep these in mind as you evaluate each expression.
2
Check for restrictive compound
A compound is restrictive if one part qualifies another, 'alladhi' can be inserted, and the whole can be expressed by a single word. Example: 'hayawan natiq' (rational animal) becomes 'insan' (human).
This also applies to adjectives like 'aswad' (black) = 'thing possessing blackness'.
3
If not restrictive, check for predicative relation
If the compound contains a nisba khabariyya (predicative relation), it is a proposition (qadiyya) and is truth-apt. Examples: 'Zayd is standing', even wish and wonder are included because their underlying meaning is a report.
4
Classify as non-informative if neither
Compounds without a predicative relation, such as commands, prohibitions, and calls, are not beneficial for acquiring knowledge. Only restrictive and predicative compounds are useful.

الفصل الأول في بيان ما هو النافع من المركبات في تحصيل المطلوبات وانحصاره في المركب التقييدي والخبري ووجه تقدم النظر في التقييدي منه على الخبري، وبيان أقسامه وتركيبه. نقول: إنا لما أتينا على بيان المفردات وأقسامها وما يتعلق بها، وجب أن نبين ما يتحصل منها من التأليف المعين في اكتساب المطلوبين - أعني التصور والتصديق - والتأليف الحاصل من المفردات، إما أن يكون من قبيل التأليف التقييدي أو ليس. فإن كان من قبيل التأليف التقييدي فهو ما بعض أجزائه متقيد ببعض، ويمكن أن تقع بينها لفظة الذي، ويعبر عن المجموع بلفظة واحدة كما في قولنا: حيوان ناطق فالحيوان مقيد بالناطق، ولولاه لكان الحيوان مطلقاً؛ ولذلك سمي (تقييداً) ويمكن أن تقع بينهما لفظة الذي فإنك إذا قلت (حيوان ناطق) كان معناه الحيوان الذي هو ناطق. ولا يخفى إمكان التعبير عن المجموع بلفظة واحدة وهي قولك إنسان، وليس هذا مختصاً بالحقائق البسيطة كالإنسان بل وهو جار في الحقائق المركبة التي ما لها فاعلان كالأسود والأبيض، إذ الأسود شيء ذو سواد، والأبيض شيء ذو بياض، ولا محالة أن أحد جزأي هذا القول وهو الشيء مقيد بالآخر وهو السواد أو البياض، ويمكن أن تقع بينهما لفظة الذي فنقول الشيء الذي هو ذو سواد أو بياض، ونعبر عن الكل بلفظ واحد وهو الأسود أو الأبيض، وأما إن لم يكن من قبيل التأليف التقييدي فإما أن يكون فيه نسبة خبرية أو ليس. فإن كان فيه نسبة خبرية فيسمى (خبراً) و(قضية) و(قولاً جازماً) ويدخل فيه التمني والتعجب، فإن حامله يرجع إلى الخبر وسيأتي الكلام في تحقيقه وتحقيق أقسامه، وإن لم يكن فيه نسبة خبرية فكالأمر والنهي، والدعاء والمسألة والنداء والاستخبار، غير أن الموصل من
The first chapter on explaining what is beneficial from compounds in acquiring the sought things, and that it is confined to the restrictive compound and the predicative compound, and the precedence of considering the restrictive over the predicative, and explaining its divisions and composition. We say: Since we have completed the explanation of simple terms and their divisions and what pertains to them, it is necessary that we explain what is obtained from them of the specific composition in acquiring the two sought things—namely, concept (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq)—and the composition obtained from simple terms is either of the type of restrictive composition or not. If it is of the type of restrictive composition, it is that whose parts are qualified by each other, and it is possible that the word 'which' (alladhi) occurs between them, and the whole is expressed by a single word, as in our saying: 'rational animal' — for 'animal' is qualified by 'rational', and without it 'animal' would be absolute; therefore it is called 'restrictive' (taqyidi). And it is possible that the word 'which' occurs between them, for if you say 'rational animal', its meaning is 'the animal that is rational'. And it is not hidden that it is possible to express the whole by a single word, which is your saying 'human' (insan). And this is not specific to simple realities like 'human', but rather it applies to compound realities that have two [descriptive] aspects like 'black' and 'white', for 'black' is a thing possessing blackness, and 'white' is a thing possessing whiteness; necessarily one of the two parts of this expression—namely 'thing'—is qualified by the other—namely 'blackness' or 'whiteness'—and it is possible that the word 'which' occurs between them, so we say 'the thing that is possessing blackness or whiteness', and we express the whole by a single word which is 'black' or 'white'. And if it is not of the type of restrictive composition, then either it contains a predicative relation or not. If it contains a predicative relation, it is called 'report' (khabar), 'proposition' (qadiyya), and 'assertoric statement' (qawl jazim), and includes wish and wonder, for their carrier returns to the report; and discussion will come on its verification and the verification of its divisions. And if it does not contain a predicative relation, then it is like command, prohibition, supplication, request, calling, and interrogation, except that the means by which...
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of compound expressions (murakkabat) in logic. The author begins by stating that after covering simple terms (mufradat), the next task is to explain the composition that yields concept (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq), the two goals of logic. The composition from simple terms is divided into two types: restrictive (taqyidi) and non-restrictive. The restrictive compound is defined as one where parts qualify each other, such that the word 'alladhi' (which) can be inserted between them, and the whole can be expressed by a single term. The prime example is 'hayawan natiq' (rational animal), which is the definition of 'insan' (human). The author emphasizes that this applies not only to simple realities like 'human' but also to derived attributes like 'aswad' (black) and 'abyad' (white), which are 'shay' dhu sawad' (thing possessing blackness) and 'shay' dhu bayad' (thing possessing whiteness). The non-restrictive compound is then divided: if it contains a predicative relation (nisba khabariyya), it is called khabar (report), qadiyya (proposition), or qawl jazim (assertoric statement), and it includes expressions of wish and wonder because their underlying meaning is a report. If it does not contain a predicative relation, it includes commands, prohibitions, supplications, requests, calls, and interrogations. The passage ends abruptly but establishes the foundational classification: only restrictive compounds (definitions) and predicative compounds (propositions) are useful for attaining knowledge (tasawwur and tasdiq), while other compounds are non-informative.

Restrictive and Predicative Compositions
Author classifies composite expressions into restrictive and predicative, explaining their epistemic roles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish restrictive from predicative composite expressions and identify which epistemic goal (tasawwur or tasdiq) each serves.

Core Idea
Restrictive composition yields conception; predicative composition yields assent.

In this passage, the author transitions from simple terms to composite expressions, dividing them into two fundamental types. Understanding this division is crucial because each type corresponds to one of the two main objectives of logic: conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). We will learn the diagnostic test for restrictive composition and see why not all theoretical conceptions come from restrictive composites.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of composition
The author states that composition is either restrictive (ta'lif taqyidi) or non-restrictive. Non-restrictive compounds are further divided into those with a predicative relation (khabari) and those without (e.g., commands). Focus on the first two.
2
Apply the 'alladhi' test for restrictiveness
A composition is restrictive if you can insert the word 'alladhi' (which) between its parts. For example, 'speaking animal' becomes 'the animal which is speaking'. This shows one part restricts the other.
The author also notes that the whole can be expressed by a single word, like 'human'.
3
Determine the epistemic output
Restrictive composition leads to conception (tasawwur). Predicative composition leads to assent (tasdiq). The author explicitly says 'the means to conception is only restrictive composition' and 'the means to assent is only the predicative composite'.
4
Recognize the caveat on conception
The author warns that not every theoretical conception is acquired from a restrictive composition. Conception can also arise from other conceptions, but in a limited way (for distinction, not complete knowledge).
This nuance prevents a common misunderstanding.

نقول : إنا لما أتينا على بيان المفردات وأقسامها وما يتعلق بها، وجب أن نبين ما يتحصل منها من التأليف المعين في اكتساب المطلوبين - أعني التصور والتصديق - والتأليف الحاصل من المفردات، إما أن يكون من قبيل التأليف التقييدي أو ليس فإن كان من قبيل التأليف التقييدي فهو ما بعض أجزائه متقيد ببعض، ويمكن أن تقع بينها لفظة الذي ، ويعبر عن المجموع بلفظة واحدة كما في قولنا: حيوان ناطق فالحيوان مقيد بالناطق، ولولاه لكان الحيوان مطلقاً؛ ولذلك سُمي (تقييداً) ويمكن أن تقع بينهما لفظة الذي فإنك إذا قلت (حيوان ناطق) كان معناه الحيوان الذي هو ناطق. ولا يخفى إمكان التعبير عن المجموع بلفظة واحدة وهي قولك إنسان، وليس هذا مختصاً بالحقائق البسيطة كالإنسان بل وهو جار في الحقائق المركبة التي ما لها فاعلان كالأسود والأبيض إذ الأسود شيء ذو سواد، والأبيض شيء ذو بياض، ولا محالة أن أحد جزأي هذا القول وهو الشيء مقيد بالآخر وهو السواد أو البياض، ويمكن أن تقع بينهما لفظة الذي فنقول الشيء الذي هو ذو سواد أو بياض، ونعبر عن الكل بلفظ واحد وهو الأسود أو الأبيض، وأما إن لم يكن من قبيل التأليف التقييدي فإما أن يكون فيه نسبة خبرية أو ليس فإن كان فيه نسبة خبرية فيسمى (خبراً) و(قضية) و(قولاً جازماً) ويدخل فيه التمني والتعجب، فإن حامله يرجع إلى الخبر وسيأتي الكلام في تحقيقه وتحقيق أقسامه، وإن لم يكن فيه نسبة خبرية فكالأمر والنهي، والدعاء والمسألة والنداء والاستخبار، غير أن الموصل من جملة ذلك كله إلى التصور إنما هو التركيب التقييدي، والموصل منه إلى التصديق إنما هو المركب الخبري، ولما كان التصور أبسط من التصديق كان أولى بالنظر في ما يتوصل إليه، وليس يلزم من كون المركب التقييدي مقيداً للتصور النظري أن ينعكس كنفسه، حتى يكون كل تصور نظري مكتسباً من المركب التقييدي، بل التصور أيضاً قد يكون مفيداً للتصور كما سنبينه بعد، لكن بالنظر إلى التمييز دون معرفة الحقيقة بكاملها
We say: When we have completed the explanation of simple terms, their divisions, and what pertains to them, it is necessary to explain what results from them of the specific composition in acquiring the two sought things—namely, conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). The composition resulting from simple terms is either of the restrictive type (al-ta'lif al-taqyidi) or not. If it is of the restrictive type, it is that wherein some of its parts are restricted by others, and the word 'which' (alladhi) can occur between them, and the whole is expressed by a single word, as in our saying 'speaking animal' (hayawan natiq); for 'animal' is restricted by 'speaking', and without it 'animal' would be absolute. Therefore it is called 'restriction'. The word 'which' can occur between them, for if you say 'speaking animal', its meaning is 'the animal which is speaking'. And it is not hidden that it is possible to express the whole by a single word, namely your saying 'human' (insan). And this is not specific to simple realities like 'human', but rather it applies to composite realities that have no agent, like 'black' (aswad) and 'white' (abyad); for 'black' is 'a thing possessing blackness', and 'white' is 'a thing possessing whiteness'. Undoubtedly, one part of this expression, namely 'thing', is restricted by the other, namely 'blackness' or 'whiteness', and the word 'which' can occur between them, so we say 'the thing which is possessing blackness or whiteness', and we express the whole by a single word, which is 'black' or 'white'. As for if it is not of the restrictive type, then either it contains a predicative relation (nisba khabariyya) or not. If it contains a predicative relation, it is called 'khabar' (predication), 'qadiyya' (proposition), and 'qawl jazim' (assertive statement), and wish (tamanni) and exclamation (ta'ajjub) fall under it, for their import reverts to the predicate—and discussion of its verification and its divisions will come later. If it does not contain a predicative relation, then it is like command, prohibition, supplication, question, call, and interrogation. However, the means from all of that to conception is only the restrictive composition, and the means from it to assent is only the predicative composite. Since conception is simpler than assent, it is more worthy of being considered first regarding what leads to it. And it does not necessarily follow from the fact that the restrictive composite restricts theoretical conception that the reverse holds as well, such that every theoretical conception is acquired from the restrictive composite. Rather, conception may also be productive of conception, as we will explain later, but with respect to distinction (tamyiz) without knowledge of the complete reality.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies composite expressions (al-ta'lif) in logic, distinguishing two main types: restrictive (taqyidi) and those with a predicative relation (khabari). Restrictive composition is where one part restricts another, like 'speaking animal' where 'speaking' restricts 'animal', and the whole can be expressed by a single word such as 'human'. The author gives examples from simple and composite realities: 'human' is a simple reality, while 'black' and 'white' are composite because they consist of 'thing' plus a property (blackness/whiteness). Non-restrictive compositions either contain a predicative relation (like propositions) or do not (like commands and prohibitions). The author then explains the epistemic role: the restrictive composite leads to conception (tasawwur), while the predicative composite leads to assent (tasdiq). Because conception is simpler, it is considered first. However, the author cautions that not every theoretical conception comes from a restrictive composite; sometimes conception can produce other conceptions, specifically for distinction (tamyiz) rather than complete knowledge. This sets the stage for the discussion of definitions that follows.

التركيب الخبري
التصور

Key Takeaway
Restrictive compositions yield conceptions; predicative compositions yield assents; this distinction underlies the structure of definition and proof in logic.

The Three Kinds of Definitions: Hadd, Rasm, and Lafdhi
Author divides definitions into three types based on the seeker's knowledge: hadd, rasm, and lafdhi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of definitions (hadd, rasm, lafdhi) based on the seeker's state of ignorance.

Core Idea
Definitions are tailored to what the questioner lacks: reality or word indication.

The author categorizes definitions according to the seeker's need. If you lack knowledge of the thing itself, you need either a real definition (hadd) or a description (rasm). If you know the thing but not the word, you need a verbal explanation (lafdhi). This framework is essential for precise conceptual analysis in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the seeker's ignorance type
Determine whether the questioner is ignorant of the reality (haqiqa) or only of the word's indication (dalala) on that reality.
The passage explicitly divides the seeker into these two categories.
2
Match the request to the definition type
If ignorant of reality, does the seeker want essential properties (dhatiyyat) or accidental (aradiyyat)? Essentials lead to hadd, accidentals to rasm.
Essential properties are genus and differentia; accidental include proper accidents or common accidents.
3
Recognize the verbal definition
If the seeker knows the reality but not the word's reference, the answer is lafdhi, which is a synonym or explanatory phrase.
The author gives the example of 'basha'r' being explained as 'insan'.
4
Apply the classification to new examples
Consider someone asking: 'I know what prayer is, but what does the word “salat” mean?' This is a lafdhi request. Another says: 'Tell me the essence of fasting.' That requires hadd or rasm depending on whether essentials or accidentals are requested.
Practice distinguishing based on the questioner's stated ignorance.

والكل يُعبر عنه باللفظ الشارح، وإن كان منه ما هو قول، ومنه ما ليس بقول وهو لا محالة ينقسم بانقسام الطالب، إذ الطالب إما أن يكون جاهلاً بالحقيقة أو بدلالة اللفظ عليها، فإن كان جاهلاً بالحقيقة فطلبه إما لتصورها بالذاتيات أو بالعرضيات، وإن لم يكن جاهلاً بالحقيقة بل بدلالة اللفظ عليها فطلبه إنما هو لشرح دلالة الاسم فقط، فالجواب عن الأول يسمى (حداً) وعن الثاني (رسماً) وعن الثالث (لفظياً). ولا مانع من إطلاق اسم الحد على كل واحد من هذه الأقسام الثلاثة بالنظر إلى الوضع اللغوي، وليس اللفظي غير تبديل اللفظ المسؤول عن كشف دلالته بلفظ مرادف له إن كان شرط أن يكون أشهر عند السائل مما سأل عنه، وبه يتحقق إذا لم يكن جاهلاً بالحقيقة بل بدلالة اللفظ عليها وضعاً، وذلك كمن سأل عن البشر الجاهل بدلالته على معنى الإنسان مع معرفته لمعنى الإنسان، ودلالة لفظ الإنسان عليه فقال (ما) (البشر) كان الجواب إنه إنسان فإن لم يكن للفظ المسؤول عنه لفظاً مُرادفاً؟ كان الكشف عنه بالعبارة الجدية أو الرسمية الدالة على الماهية الموضوعة للفظ المسؤول عنه، وذلك كمن سأل عن شرح اسم البشر وقدر مثلاً عدم مرادفة لفظ الإنسان له أو غيره من الألفاظ كان الجواب الشارح له أنه الحيوان الناطق أو الضاحك أو غير ذلك من الأقوال الشارحة، فهذان الجوابان وإن كان أحدهما عند طلب التمييز بالذاتيات يسمى (حداً) أو الآخر عند طلب التمييز بالعرضيات يسمى (رسماً) كما يأتي، فهو عند العلم بالحقيقة والطلب لشرح اسمها لا يكون إلا لفظاً، ولما كان اللفظي سهل التناول على قاصديه فربما كره الأطناب فيه، وكان الواجب صرف العناية إلى تحقيق القسمين الأولين لعسرهما، وعند ذلك نقول الحد والرس
And all of that is expressed by the explanatory expression (al-lafz al-sharih), although part of it is a statement (qawl) and part is not a statement. And it is inevitably divided according to the division of the seeker (talib). For the seeker is either ignorant of the reality (haqiqa) or ignorant of the linguistic indication (dalala) of the word on it. If he is ignorant of the reality, his request is either to conceive it by its essential properties (dhatiyyat) or by its accidental properties (aradiyyat). If he is not ignorant of the reality but rather of the linguistic indication of the word on it, then his request is only to explain the indication of the name. So the answer to the first is called 'hadd' (definition), to the second 'rasm' (description), and to the third 'lafdhi' (verbal). There is no objection to applying the name 'hadd' to each of these three divisions from the perspective of linguistic usage. And the verbal (lafdhi) is nothing but replacing the word asked about to reveal its indication with a synonym for it, provided that it is more well-known to the questioner than what he asked about. It is realized when he is not ignorant of the reality but rather of the linguistic indication of the word upon it by convention. That is like one who asks about 'basha'r' (humankind) while ignorant of its indication of the meaning of 'insan' (human) despite knowing the meaning of 'insan' and the indication of the word 'insan' on it. He says 'What is basha'r?' The answer is that it is 'insan'. If there is no synonym for the word asked about, then the disclosure is through the essential (jinsi) or descriptive (rasmi) expression that indicates the quiddity (mahiyya) specified for the word asked about. That is like one who asks for an explanation of the name 'basha'r' and supposes, for example, that there is no synonym of the word 'insan' for it or other words; the explanatory answer for him is that it is 'the speaking animal' or 'the laughing one' or other explanatory statements. These two answers, even if one of them in the case of requesting distinction by essential properties is called 'hadd' and the other in the case of requesting distinction by accidental properties is called 'rasm' as will come, in the case of knowing the reality and requesting an explanation of its name, it is only verbal (lafdhi). Since the verbal is easy to grasp for its seekers, perhaps prolixity is disliked in it, and it is necessary to direct attention to the verification of the first two divisions due to their difficulty. At that point we say: the hadd and the rasm...
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage classifies definitions into three types based on what the questioner lacks. The seeker may be ignorant of the reality itself (haqiqa) or only of the linguistic indication (dalala) of a word. If ignorant of the reality, he may want to conceive it through essential properties (dhatiyyat) or accidental properties (aradiyyat). The answer in the first case is 'hadd' (definition by genus and differentia), and in the second, 'rasm' (description by properties). If the seeker knows the reality but not the word's reference, the answer is 'lafdhi' (verbal explanation), which usually involves providing a synonym. The author gives examples: if someone asks about 'basha'r' not knowing it means 'insan', the answer is 'insan'. If no synonym exists, one must use a hadd or rasm. The author notes that all three are sometimes called 'hadd' in linguistic usage, but technically they differ. The verbal definition is easy, so effort should be focused on the first two, which are harder. The text breaks off but clearly introduces the division.

Key Takeaway

Hadd and Rasm

The Verbal Definition (Lafdhi) in Detail
Author divides definitions into three types based on the seeker's knowledge: hadd, rasm, and lafdhi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the verbal definition and explain its procedure when a synonym is available and when it is not.

Core Idea
The verbal definition simply replaces an unknown word with a known synonym or an explanatory phrase.

When you already understand the concept but not the word that denotes it, the definition needed is purely verbal. The author explains how this works, including cases where no synonym exists. Understanding this is crucial for precise terminology in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the condition for lafdhi
The seeker knows the reality (haqiqa) but is ignorant of the word's conventional indication (dalala wad'iyya). His request is to explain the name, not the quiddity.
The passage says: 'إذا لم يكن جاهلاً بالحقيقة بل بدلالة اللفظ عليها وضعاً'.
2
Apply the synonym rule
If a more familiar synonym exists, the answer is to provide that synonym. The author's example: someone knows 'insan' but not 'basha'r' – answer is 'basha'r is insan'.
The synonym must be more well-known to the questioner.
3
Handle missing synonym
If no synonym exists, one must use an essential or descriptive expression (hadd or rasm) that indicates the quiddity, but the purpose remains explaining the word, not the quiddity.
For example, if 'basha'r' had no synonym, one might say 'the speaking animal' (hadd) or 'the laughing one' (rasm) as a verbal explanation.
4
Differentiate lafdhi from hadd/rasm
Even if the same words are used, the intent differs: in lafdhi, you assume the seeker already knows the quiddity; the explanation is purely to link word to meaning.
The author stresses: 'فهو عند العلم بالحقيقة والطلب لشرح اسمها لا يكون إلا لفظاً'.

Hadd and Rasm

Three Types of Explanatory Answers: Hadd, Rasm, Lafdhi
Author classifies requests into three types and defines corresponding answers: hadd, rasm, lafdhi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given question as seeking hadd, rasm, or lafdhi based on the seeker's ignorance.

Core Idea
The answer type depends on what the seeker lacks: quiddity (hadd/rasm) or word meaning (lafdhi).

This passage lays the foundation for all subsequent discussions on definitions in Islamic logic. It divides explanatory statements according to the seeker's need: ignorance of the quiddity (essential or accidental) or ignorance of the word's meaning. Understanding this classification is crucial for correctly identifying the type of definition required in Islamic sciences, whether in fiqh, usul, or kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the seeker's state
Determine whether the seeker is ignorant of the quiddity itself or only of the word's denotation. This is the primary division.
2
Distinguish subtypes of quiddity ignorance
If ignorant of the quiddity, note whether the request is for essential attributes (dhatiyat) or accidental attributes ('ardiyat). This distinguishes hadd from rasm.
3
Match the answer type
Assign the answer: hadd for essential quiddity, rasm for accidental/mixed, lafdhi for verbal explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جملة ذلك كله إلى التصور إنما هو التركيب التقييدي، والموصل منه إلى التصديق إنما هو المركب الخبري، ولما كان التصور أبسط من التصديق كان أولى بالنظر في ما يتوصل إليه، وليس يلزم من كون المركب التقييدي مقيداً للتصور النظري أن ينعكس كنفسه، حتى يكون كل تصور نظري مكتسباً من المركب التقييدي، بل التصور أيضاً قد يكون مفيداً للتصور كما سنبينه بعد، لكن بالنظر إلى التمييز دون معرفة الحقيقة بكاملها، والكل يُعبر عنه باللفظ الشارح، وإن كان منه ما هو قول، ومنه ما ليس بقول وهو لا محالة ينقسم بانقسام الطالب، إذ الطالب إما أن يكون جاهلاً بالحقيقة أو بدلالة اللفظ عليها، فإن كان جاهلاً بالحقيقة فطلبه إما لتصورها بالذاتيات أو بالعرضيات، وإن لم يكن جاهلاً بالحقيقة بل بدلالة اللفظ عليها فطلبه إنما هو لشرح دلالة الاسم فقط، فالجواب عن الأول يسمى (حداً) وعن الثاني (رسماً) وعن الثالث (لفظياً).
The summary of all that: reaching conception (tasawwur) is through the restrictive compound (al-tarkib al-taqyidi), and what reaches assent (tasdiq) is the predicative compound (al-murakkab al-khabari). Since conception is simpler than assent, it is more appropriate to consider what leads to it. It does not follow from the restrictive compound being restrictive of theoretical conception that it reflects itself, such that every theoretical conception is acquired from the restrictive compound. Rather, conception may also be beneficial for conception as we will later explain, but with a view to distinction (tamyiz) without knowledge of the complete quiddity. All of that is expressed by the explanatory expression (al-lafz al-sharih), although some of it is a statement (qawl) and some is not. It necessarily divides according to the division of the seeker: for the seeker is either ignorant of the quiddity or of the indication of the word upon it. If he is ignorant of the quiddity, his request is either for conceiving it by the essential (dhatiyat) or accidental ('ardiyat). If he is not ignorant of the quiddity but of the indication of the word upon it, his request is only for explaining the signification of the name. Thus, the answer to the first is called 'hadd' (definition), to the second 'rasm' (description), and to the third 'lafdhi' (verbal).
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a foundational classification in logic: it divides the explanatory statements (al-aqwal al-shariha) that fulfill the purpose of conception (tasawwur). The author begins by noting that the restrictive compound (tarkib taqyidi) leads to conception, while the predicative compound (murakkab khabari) leads to assent. Conception is simpler, so it is proper to start with it. However, not every conception is obtained through a restrictive compound; some conceptions help distinguish without giving full quiddity. The key division comes from the state of the seeker: if he is ignorant of the quiddity itself, he may request definition by essential or accidental attributes; if he only ignores the word's meaning, he asks for a verbal explanation. The answers correspond respectively: hadd (definition by essential genus and differentia), rasm (description by essential and accidental or only accidental), and lafdhi (verbal explanation by synonym). This classification is the blueprint for all subsequent discussions on definitions.

لفظي

D) Both hadd and rasm

Key Takeaway
The seeker's ignorance determines the type of explanatory answer: essential attributes for hadd, accidental or mixed for rasm, and word meaning for lafdhi.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Hadd and Rasm

Understanding the Lafdhi (Verbal) Definition
Author explains linguistic extension of 'hadd' and details lafdhi definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a lafdhi definition is appropriate and correctly apply it in Islamic terminological contexts.

Core Idea
A lafdhi definition replaces an unknown word with a more famous synonym; otherwise, it uses hadd/rasm as verbal explanation.

In the previous lesson, we distinguished three types of definition: haqiqi, lafdhi, and rasm. Now we focus on the lafdhi definition. The author explains that, linguistically, 'hadd' can cover all three, but technically lafdhi is distinct. He details its conditions and procedure, which is crucial for correctly answering questions about word meanings in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the seeker's state
Determine whether the person asking is ignorant of the quiddity (essence) or merely ignorant of the word's indication. The lafdhi definition applies only when they know the quiddity but not the word that signifies it.
2
Check for a synonym more famous than the original
If a synonym exists that is more familiar to the asker, the definition is simply stating that synonym. For example, 'al-bashar' is answered with 'al-insan' because these are synonyms and 'insan' is more common.
3
If no synonym exists, use a real definition verbally
When the asked word has no synonym, you must use a hadd (essential definition) or a rasm (descriptive definition) to explain the meaning. Even though it is a real definition, the purpose is still verbal: to explain the name, not the quiddity.
4
Distinguish function from form
The same expression can serve as a lafdhi definition in one context and as a hadd or rasm in another. The key is the seeker's need: if they already know the essence but not the word, it's lafdhi; if they don't know the essence, it's haqiqi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا مانع من إطلاق اسم الحد على كل واحد من هذه الأقسام الثلاثة بالنظر إلى الوضع اللغوي، وليس اللفظي غير تبديل اللفظ المسؤول عن كشف دلالته بلفظ مرادف له إن كان شرط أن يكون أشهر عند السائل مما سأل عنه، وبه يتحقق إذا لـم يكن جاهلاً بالحقيقة بل بدلالة اللفظ عليها وضعاً، وذلك كمن سأل عن البشر الجاهل بدلالته على معنى الإنسان مع معرفته لمعنى الإنسان، ودلالة لفظ الإنسان عليه فقال (ما) (البشر) كان الجواب إنه إنسان فإن لم يكن للفظ المسؤول عنه لفظاً مُرادفاً؟ كان الكشف عنه بالعبارة الجدية أو الرسمية الدالة على الماهية الموضوعة للفظ المسؤول عنه، وذلك كمن سأل عن شرح اسم البشر وقدر مثلاً عدم مرادفة لفظ الإنسان له أو غيره من الألفاظ كان الجواب الشارح له أنه الحيوان الناطق أو الضاحك أو غير ذلك من الأقوال الشارحة، فهذان الجوابان وإن كان أحدهما عند طلب التمييز بالذاتيات يسمى (حداً) أو الآخر عند طلب التمييز بالعرضيات يسمى (رسماً) كما يأتي، فهو عند العلم بالحقيقة والطلب لشرح اسمها لا يكون إلا لفظاً.
There is no obstacle to applying the name 'hadd' to each of these three divisions from the perspective of linguistic convention. The verbal (lafdhi) is nothing but substituting the asked word for its indication with a synonymous word, provided it is more famous to the asker than what he asked about. This is realized when he is not ignorant of the quiddity but of the indication of the word upon it by convention. For example, if someone asks about 'al-bashar' (mankind) being ignorant of its indication of the meaning of 'insan' while knowing the meaning of 'insan' and the indication of the word 'insan' upon it, and he says 'What is al-bashar?', the answer is 'It is insan.' If the asked word has no synonym, then the uncovering is done by either an essential (jadhiyya) or descriptive (rasmiyya) expression that indicates the quiddity posited for the asked word. For example, if someone asks for an explanation of the name 'al-bashar' and there happens to be no synonym for 'insan' for it, the explanatory answer would be 'it is the rational animal' or 'the laughing animal' or other explanatory statements. These two answers, although one in the case of requesting distinction by essentials is called 'hadd' and the other in the case of requesting distinction by accidentals is called 'rasm' as will come, when there is knowledge of the quiddity and the request is for explaining its name, it is nothing but verbal.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage expands on the previous classification. The author first notes that it is linguistically permissible to call all three types 'hadd', but technically they are distinct. He then details the lafdhi definition: it is simply replacing the unknown word with a more familiar synonym. The condition is that the synonym is more famous to the asker. If a synonym exists, the answer is straightforward; if not, one must use a real definition (hadd or rasm) but the purpose remains verbal — to explain the name, not the quiddity. The example given is 'al-bashar' and 'al-insan'. In Arabic, 'bashar' is synonymous with 'insan', so 'insan' answers the question. But if no synonym existed, the answer would be the true definition 'hayawan natiq' or the description 'hayawan dahik', as in the case of 'al-bashar' lacking a synonym. This emphasizes that the same expression can serve different functions depending on the seeker's state.

Key Terms
لفظي
مرادف
العبارة الجدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
A lafdhi definition is a verbal substitution of an unknown word with a more famous synonym, or if no synonym exists, using a real definition (hadd or rasm) for the purpose of explaining the name, not the essence.
